Requirement of a Mahram for Women's Hajj
Definition and rulings on mahram for women's Hajj

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify who qualifies as a mahram, apply the conditions for its requirement in Hajj, and distinguish exceptions (slave women, short distances, husband's permission).

Core Idea
A woman's Hajj over three days requires a mahram or husband; the mahram is anyone with whom marriage is permanently forbidden.

In this passage, Al-Quduri and Al-Nayra clarify a key condition for women performing Hajj: the presence of a mahram or husband. The commentator expands on the definition of mahram, listing who qualifies and who does not, and also explains the distance threshold and exceptions. Understanding these details ensures that a woman's Hajj obligation is correctly assessed.

Guided Path
1
Define mahram and list its conditions
Start by identifying the definition of mahram as given: anyone with whom marriage is permanently prohibited (haram mu'abbad), whether by blood, affinity, or fosterage.
This includes free men, slaves, and dhimmis, but excludes Magians.
2
Identify who is and is not a mahram
Apply the definition to specific cases: minor boys, insane persons, and a woman's own slave are not mahram. A near-adolescent (murahiq) is treated as an adult.
The key distinction is permanent prohibition; a slave's prohibition is not permanent because he may become free and marry her.
3
Apply the distance condition (3 days or more)
The mahram requirement applies only if the distance to Makkah is three days or more. If less, she may travel without a mahram or husband, unless she is in iddah.
The distance is measured as travel time, not literal miles.
4
Note exceptions and the husband's permission
Slave women (including mudabbarah, umm walad, mukatabah) are exempt from the mahram requirement. For obligatory Hajj, the husband cannot prevent her if she has a mahram; for supererogatory Hajj, he may prevent her.
If no mahram exists, she is not obligated to marry or earn for the journey.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
* قوله: (ويُعتبر في المرأة أن يكون لها مَحْرَم يَحجُّ بها، أو زوج)،
سواء كانت عجوزاً، أو شابةً.
وهو: كل من لا يجوز له مناكحتها على التأبيد، سواء كان بالرحم، أو بالمصاهرة، أو بالرضاع، وسواء كان حراً، أو عبداً، أو ذمياً.
وأما المجوسي: فليس بمَحرَم.
والصبي والمجنون: ليس بمحرم.
والمراهق : كالبالغ.
وعبد المرأة: ليس بمَحرَم لها ; لأن تحريم نكاحها عليه ليس على التأبيد، بدليل أنها إذا أعتقته : جاز له نكاحها.
والصبية التي تشتهى : كالبالغة.
والأمة والمدبرة وأم الولد والمكاتبة يجوز لهن السفر بغير محرم.
والمحرم إنما يُعتبر إذا كان بينها وبين مكة ثلاثة أيام، فصاعداً، وأما إذا كان أقل : فعليها أن تخرج للحج بغير محرم، ولا زوج.
إلا أن تكون معتدةً، فلا تخرج حتى تنقضي عدتُها.
وأما إذا لم يكن للمرأة محرم، ولا زوج: لم يجب عليها أن تتزوج بمن يحج بها، كما لا يجب عليها اكتساب الراحلة.
- ثم إذا كان لها مَحرَم تخرج لحجة الفرض وإن لم يأذن لها زوجها ; لأن حق الزوج لا يظهر في حق الفرائض، وأما في التطوع والمنذور: فله منعها.
His saying: (And it is considered for the woman that she has a mahram to perform Hajj with her, or a husband), whether she is old or young. And a mahram is: every person to whom marriage is permanently prohibited, whether by blood, affinity, or fosterage, and whether free, slave, or dhimmi. As for a Magian, he is not a mahram. A minor boy and an insane person are not mahram. A near-adolescent (murahiq) is like an adult. A woman's slave is not a mahram for her because the prohibition of marriage with him is not permanent; proof is that if she frees him, it becomes permissible for him to marry her. A young girl who is desired is like an adult. A slave woman, a mudabbarah, an umm walad, and a mukatabah are allowed to travel without a mahram. The mahram is only considered if the distance between her and Makkah is three days or more; if less, she must go out for Hajj without a mahram or husband. Except if she is in iddah, then she does not go out until her iddah expires. And if a woman has no mahram or husband, she is not obligated to marry someone who can perform Hajj with her, just as she is not obligated to acquire a mount. Then if she has a mahram, she goes out for the obligatory Hajj even if her husband does not give permission, because the husband's right does not apply to obligatory acts; but in supererogatory and vowed Hajj, he may prevent her.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage defines and elaborates on the requirement of a mahram for women performing Hajj. The matn states that a woman must have a mahram or husband to accompany her. The commentator explains: this applies to all women, old or young. A mahram is defined as any person with whom marriage is permanently forbidden (haram mu'abbad), whether due to blood relation, marriage (affinity), or fosterage. This includes free men, slaves, and dhimmis. Exceptions: a Magian (Majusi) is not a mahram because his marriage prohibition is not permanent in Islamic law. A minor boy and an insane person cannot serve as mahram. A near-adolescent (murahiq) is treated as an adult. A woman's own slave is not a mahram because the prohibition on marriage is not permanent (if freed, marriage becomes halal). A young girl who is sexually desirable is like an adult in requiring a mahram. Slave women (including mudabbarah, umm walad, mukatabah) are allowed to travel without a mahram. The mahram requirement applies only if the distance to Makkah is three days or more; if less, she may go without a mahram or husband unless she is in iddah. If a woman has no mahram or husband, she is not required to marry just to perform Hajj, nor to earn money for a mount. Importantly, if she has a mahram, she may go for obligatory Hajj even without the husband's permission, but for voluntary or vowed Hajj, the husband may prevent her.

Key Terms
مَحْرَم (mahram): A person with whom marriage is permanently prohibited (haram mu'abbad), e.g., father, brother, son, etc.
عبد المرأة (abdu al-mar'ah): The female's own male slave; not a mahram because marriage prohibition is not permanent (he may become free and marry her).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is considered a valid mahram for a woman performing Hajj?
A) Her adult Muslim son
B) Her male slave
C) A Magian (Majusi)
D) A 12-year-old boy

Key Takeaway
A woman's Hajj over three days requires a mahram or husband; the mahram must be an adult, sane person with permanent marriage prohibition, except for slave women who are exempt.

Definition of Mahram and Who Qualifies as Mahram
Defining mahram and listing qualifications and exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of mahram and identify which individuals qualify and which do not.

Core Idea
A mahram is someone with whom marriage is permanently forbidden—by blood, marriage, or suckling—excluding Majusi, minors, the insane, and a woman's own slave.

This passage from Al-Jawhara al-Nayyira explains the legal concept of mahram, essential for determining who may accompany a woman on travel for Hajj. The definition hinges on the permanence of marriage prohibition, and the author lists several specific exceptions that every Hanafi must know.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basis of mahram
The core condition is a permanent prohibition of marriage. Note the three categories: blood, marriage, and suckling.
2
Recognize the inclusive scope
The mahram can be free or slave, and can be a Muslim or a dhimmi (non-Muslim under Muslim protection). The exception is the Majusi (Zoroastrian), who is not a mahram.
Dhimmi is accepted; Majusi is not.
3
Apply the exceptions
A minor boy (who has not reached puberty) and an insane person are not mahram. However, a boy approaching puberty (murahiq, around 15) is treated like an adult.
Murahiq is considered fully capable.
4
Distinguish the slave case
A woman's own slave is not her mahram because the prohibition of marriage can be lifted if she frees him. The prohibition is not permanent.
This contrasts with permanent prohibitions like blood relations.
5
Extend to the young girl
A young girl who is desired sexually (i.e., attractive) requires a mahram like an adult woman, even if she hasn't reached puberty.
The need for a mahram is based on desirability, not just age.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سواء كانت عجوزاً، أو شابةً. وهو: كل من لا يجوز له مناكحتها على التأبيد، سواء كان بالرحم، أو بالمصاهرة، أو بالرضاع، وسواء كان حراً، أو عبداً، أو ذمياً. وأما المجوسي: فليس بمَحرَم. والصبي والمجنون: ليس بمحرم. والمراهق : كالبالغ. وعبد المرأة: ليس بمَحرَم لها ؛ لأن تحريم نكاحها عليه ليس على التأبيد، بدليل أنها إذا أعتقته : جاز له نكاحها. والصبية التي تشتهى : كالبالغة.
Whether she is old or young. And he (mahram) is: everyone for whom marriage with her is forbidden permanently, whether due to blood, marriage, or suckling, and whether he is free, a slave, or a dhimmi. As for the Majusi: he is not a mahram. The minor boy and the insane: they are not mahram. The boy approaching puberty: like an adult. The slave of a woman: is not her mahram, because the prohibition of marriage to him is not permanent, as evidence that if she frees him, it is permissible for him to marry her. And the young girl who is desired: like an adult woman.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'mahram' in the context of a woman's travel for Hajj. It states that anyone with whom marriage is permanently forbidden—due to blood kinship, marriage relationship, or suckling—is a mahram, regardless of his status (free, slave, or dhimmi). It then lists exceptions: a Majusi (Zoroastrian) is not a mahram; a minor boy or an insane person is not a mahram; but a boy approaching puberty is considered like an adult. A woman's own slave is not her mahram because the marriage prohibition can be lifted if she frees him. A young girl who is sexually desirable is like an adult woman in requiring a mahram. This classification determines who may accompany a woman on Hajj without the risk of sin.

Key Terms
محرم (mahram): A male relative with whom marriage is permanently prohibited due to blood, marriage, or suckling; such a person may accompany a woman on travel.
مجوسي (majusi): A follower of Zoroastrianism; not considered a mahram even if a relative.
مراهق (murahiq): A boy who has nearly reached puberty; treated like an adult for mahram purposes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi text, which of the following is NOT considered a mahram?
A) Her father
B) Her adult son
C) Her male slave
D) Her brother from suckling

Key Takeaway
A mahram is defined by permanent prohibition of marriage; individuals with temporary prohibition (like a slave) or lacking capacity (minor, insane) are not mahram.

The Mahram Requirement for Women's Hajj Travel
Ruling on mahram requirement with distance condition and husband's permission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a woman must have a mahram for Hajj and identify the exceptions for slave women and short distances.

Core Idea
A woman needs a mahram only when travel is three days or more; otherwise she may go without.

This lesson clarifies the foundational ruling on women's travel for Hajj regarding the mahram. The Hanafi school specifies that the mahram requirement depends on the distance and the woman's status. Understanding these distinctions is essential for proper fiqh application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine the ruling on a woman traveling for Hajj. The text distinguishes between different scenarios: slave women, distance, and husband's permission.
2
Determine the distance condition
The mahram is required only if the distance to Makkah is three days or more (three days and nights). For shorter distances, she may go without a mahram or husband.
The hadith evidence is not in this part; it appears later.
3
Apply the exceptions
Slave women (regular, mudabbara, umm walad, mukataba) are exempt. Also, if the distance is less than three days, no mahram needed. If she is in iddah, she must wait.
4
Consider the husband's right
For obligatory Hajj, the husband cannot prevent his wife if she has a mahram. For voluntary or vowed Hajj, he may prevent her.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأمة والمدبرة وأم الولد والمكاتبة يجوز لهن السفر بغير محرم. والمحرم إنما يُعتبر إذا كان بينها وبين مكة ثلاثة أيام، فصاعداً، وأما إذا كان أقل : فعليها أن تخرج للحج بغير محرم، ولا زوج. إلا أن تكون معتدةً، فلا تخرج حتى تنقضي عدتُها. وأما إذا لم يكن للمرأة محرم، ولا زوج: لم يجب عليها أن تتزوج بمن يحج بها، كما لا يجب عليها اكتساب الراحلة. ثم إذا كان لها مَحرَم تخرج لحجة الفرض وإن لم يأذن لها زوجها ؛ لأن حق الزوج لا يظهر في حق الفرائض، وأما في التطوع والمنذور: فله منعها. ولا يجوز لها أن تحج مع غيرهما إذا كان بينها وبين مكة مسيرة ثلاثة أيام ولياليها، فصاعداً. ويجب عليها نفقة المحرم، هو الصحيح؛ لأنها لا تتوصل إلى الحج إلا به ، كما يلزمها شراء الراحلة التي لا تتوصل إلا بها. وفي الخُجندي»: لا يجب عليها ذلك. والتوفيق بينهما : أن المَحرَم إذا قال: لا أخرج إلا بالنفقة وجـ عليها، وإن خرج من غير اشتراط ذلك : لم يجب عليها. قوله: (ولا يجوز لها أن تحج بغيرهما إذا كان بينها وبين مكة مسيرة ثلاثة أيام ولياليها، فصاعداً). لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: «لا تحُجَّنَّ امرأةٌ إلا ومعها محرم». ولأنها بدون المَحرَم يُخاف عليها الفتنة، وتزداد بانضمام غيرها إليها، ولهذا تحرم الخلوة بالأجنبية وإن كان معها غيرها. هكذا في «الهداية». فإن حجت بغير مَحرَم أو زوج: جاز حجها، مع الكراهة. وهل المحرم من شرائط الوجوب، أم من شرائط الأداء؟ على الخلاف في أمن الطريق.
And the female slave, the mudabbara, the umm walad, and the mukataba: it is permissible for them to travel without a mahram. The mahram is only considered if between her and Makkah is three days or more; but if it is less, then she must go out for Hajj without a mahram and without a husband. Unless she is in iddah, then she does not go out until her iddah ends. And if a woman has neither a mahram nor a husband: it is not obligatory upon her to marry someone to perform Hajj with her, just as it is not obligatory upon her to earn a riding animal. Then, if she has a mahram, she goes out for obligatory Hajj even if her husband does not permit; because the husband's right does not appear with regard to obligations. As for voluntary and vowed Hajj, he may prevent her. And it is not permissible for her to perform Hajj with other than them (mahram or husband) if between her and Makkah is a distance of three days and nights or more. And she must bear the expenses of the mahram; this is the correct view, because she cannot reach Hajj except through him, just as she is obliged to buy a riding animal without which she cannot reach. And in al-Khujandi: it is not obligatory upon her. The reconciliation between them is: if the mahram says 'I will not go out except with expenses,' it becomes obligatory upon her; if he goes out without stipulating that, it is not obligatory upon her. His statement: 'And it is not permissible for her to perform Hajj with other than them if between her and Makkah is a distance of three days and nights or more' because of the saying of the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'No woman should perform Hajj except with a mahram.' And because without a mahram, there is fear of fitna for her, and it increases when others join her, and for this reason seclusion with a non-mahram woman is forbidden even if others are present with her. This is as in al-Hidayah. If she performs Hajj without a mahram or husband: her Hajj is valid, with dislike. And is the mahram a condition of obligation or a condition of performance? It depends on the difference regarding safety of the road.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage presents the rulings on a woman's need for a mahram when traveling for Hajj. It begins with exceptions for slave women (regular slave, mudabbara, umm walad, mukataba) who may travel without a mahram. The mahram requirement is only relevant when the distance to Makkah is three days (or nights) or more; for shorter distances, she may go without mahram or husband unless she is in iddah. If she has no mahram or husband, she is not obligated to marry or acquire a riding animal. A husband cannot prevent his wife from performing obligatory Hajj if she has a mahram, but he can prevent voluntary or vowed Hajj. For journeys of three days or more, traveling without mahram or husband is not permissible (makruh tahrimi), but the Hajj itself is valid, though disliked. The passage then addresses the mahram's expenses: the correct view is that the woman must pay them, by analogy with purchasing a riding animal. Al-Khujandi held it is not obligatory, but the reconciliation is that if the mahram stipulates expenses, she must pay; otherwise not. The hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him) is cited as evidence: 'No woman should perform Hajj except with a mahram.' Rational reasoning is also given: fear of fitna (temptation) and the danger of seclusion. Finally, it discusses whether the mahram is a condition of obligation (wujub) or of performance (ada); the answer depends on the safety of the road.

Key Terms
محرم (mahram): A male relative with whom marriage is permanently prohibited, who may accompany a woman on travel.
راحلة (raahilah): A riding animal (mount) necessary for travel; used as an analogy for the mahram's expenses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When must a woman have a mahram or husband to perform Hajj according to this passage?
A) Only if she is a slave
B) If the distance to Makkah is three days or more
C) Always, regardless of distance
D) Only if she is not in iddah

Key Takeaway
A woman's need for a mahram for Hajj depends on distance: three days or more requires one, with exceptions for slave women and short journeys.

The Obligation of Mahram's Expenses for Hajj
Intra-Hanafi dispute on mahram's expenses with reconciliation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on mahram's expenses and apply the reconciliation between the two views.

Core Idea
The mahram's expenses are obligatory if he stipulates them; otherwise they are not.

This lesson delves into a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether a woman must pay for her mahram's travel expenses when he accompanies her for Hajj. The correct view and its practical reconciliation are presented.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
The correct view (as per the text) is that the woman must pay the mahram's expenses.
The text says 'هو الصحيح' (this is the correct view).
2
Identify the opposing view
Al-Khujandi held that it is not obligatory upon her.
3
Understand the proof for the correct view
Analogy (qiyas): just as she must buy a riding animal to reach Hajj, she must pay the mahram's expenses because she cannot reach Hajj without him.
The 'illa' is necessity: she cannot accomplish the journey without the mahram.
4
Apply the reconciliation
If the mahram stipulates expenses as a condition for accompanying her, then it becomes obligatory on her. If he does not stipulate, it is not obligatory.
This resolves the dispute practically.

Hajj Without Mahram: Proof and Validity
Proof for mahram requirement and validity of Hajj without it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the evidence for the mahram requirement and determine the validity of Hajj performed without a mahram.

Core Idea
Hajj without mahram is valid but makruh tahrimi; the hadith establishes prohibition, not nullity.

This lesson examines the primary proof for the mahram requirement—the hadith of the Prophet—and the rational reasoning. It also addresses the practical outcome: if a woman performs Hajj without a mahram, her Hajj is valid but disliked.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dalil
The hadith: لا تحُجَّنَّ امرأةٌ إلا ومعها محرم (No woman should perform Hajj except with a mahram). This is the primary evidence.
The text cites it from Al-Hidayah.
2
Analyze the dalil's implication
The negative imperative indicates prohibition (tahrim). However, in Hanafi usul, a negative imperative implies prohibition but does not necessarily nullify the act if done.
The text concludes that Hajj is valid with karahah.
3
Apply the rational argument
Fear of fitna (temptation) for the woman, and the danger increases when others join her. This is analogous to the prohibition of khalwah (seclusion) with a non-mahram.
4
Determine the practical ruling
If she performs Hajj without mahram or husband, the Hajj is valid but makruh tahrimi. She does not need to repeat it.
5
Examine the condition of wujub vs ada
The text raises: is mahram a condition of obligation (shart al-wujub) or a condition of performance (shart al-ada)? It depends on the safety of the road.
This is a further ikhtilaf not fully resolved here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

A husband cannot prevent his wife from performing obligatory Hajj if she has a mahram, but he can prevent voluntary or vowed Hajj. For journeys of three days or more, traveling without mahram or husband is not permissible (makruh tahrimi), but the Hajj itself is valid, though disliked. The passage then addresses the mahram's expenses: the correct view is that the woman must pay them, by analogy with purchasing a riding animal. Al-Khujandi held it is not obligatory, but the reconciliation is that if the mahram stipulates expenses, she must pay; otherwise not. The hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him) is cited as evidence: 'No woman should perform Hajj except with a mahram.' Rational reasoning is also given: fear of fitna (temptation) and the danger of seclusion. Finally, it discusses whether the mahram is a condition of obligation (wujub) or of performance (ada); the answer depends on the safety of the road.

Key Terms
محرم (mahram): A male relative with whom marriage is permanently prohibited, who may accompany a woman on travel.
فتنة (fitnah): Temptation, trial, or discord. Here, fear of moral transgression due to absence of a mahram.
خلوة (khalwah): Seclusion with a non-mahram, which is prohibited.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on the validity of Hajj performed without a mahram according to this passage?
A) It is invalid and must be repeated.
B) It is valid but disliked (makruh).
C) It is valid and fully acceptable.
D) It is invalid unless she repents.

Key Takeaway
Hajj without a mahram is valid but makruh tahrimi; the hadith establishes prohibition, not nullity.

Woman's Hajj: Mahram and Expenses
Ruling with proof on mahram and expenses for woman's Hajj

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the mahram requirement for a woman's Hajj, explain the ruling on his expenses, and distinguish between condition of obligation (shart al-wujub) and condition of performance (shart al-ada).

Core Idea
A woman's Hajj without mahram is valid but disliked if the road is safe; mahram's expenses are obligatory only if he stipulates them.

This passage from al-Jawhara al-Nayirah clarifies the Hanafi ruling on the mahram requirement for a woman's Hajj and the financial obligation towards the mahram. It presents the proof from hadith, the fear of fitnah, and an internal Hanafi disagreement over expenses, resolved by a condition-based distinction. The passage also introduces the crucial usul distinction between shart al-wujub and shart al-ada, which determines whether Hajj without a mahram is valid or not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mahram requirement
The text states it is not permissible for a woman to perform Hajj without her husband or mahram if the distance is three days or more. Determine what qualifies as a mahram.
The hadith 'La tahujjanna imra'atan illa wa ma'aha mahram' is the proof.
2
Extract the ruling on expenses
The text specifies that if the mahram demands expenses, she must pay them; otherwise not. This reconciles two views: the preferred view (obligatory) and al-Khujandi's (not obligatory).
The phrase 'huwa al-sahih' indicates the relied-upon position.
3
Distinguish shart al-wujub vs shart al-ada
When the road is safe, the mahram is a condition of performance (shart al-ada), meaning Hajj without him is valid but disliked. When unsafe, it is a condition of obligation (shart al-wujub), meaning the Hajj is not obligatory without him.
This determines whether the act is valid or not.
4
State the consequence of going without mahram
If the road is safe and she goes without a mahram, her Hajj is valid (jaza) but disliked (ma'a al-karahah).
This confirms it is not a condition of obligation.

It is not permissible for her to perform Hajj with other than them [her husband or mahram] if between her and Makkah there is a distance of three days and nights or more. And the expense of the mahram is obligatory upon her, this is the correct view; because she cannot reach the Hajj except through him, just as the purchase of a mount which she cannot reach except with it is obligatory upon her. - And in al-Khujandi: it is not obligatory upon her. And the reconciliation between them is: if the mahram says, 'I will not go out except with expenses,' then it is obligatory upon her; but if he goes out without stipulating that, then it is not obligatory upon her. His statement: (And it is not permissible for her to perform Hajj with other than them if between her and [Makkah] there is a distance of three days and nights or more). Makkah distance? Because of his statement, peace be upon him: 'A woman must not perform Hajj unless with a mahram.' And because without a mahram, fitnah is feared for her, and it increases when others accompany her, and for this reason seclusion with a non-mahram woman is prohibited even if others are with her. This is in al-Hidayah. So if she performs Hajj without a mahram or husband, her Hajj is valid with dislike. And is the mahram a condition of obligation or a condition of performance? It depends on the difference regarding the safety of the road.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage addresses the requirement of a mahram for a woman performing Hajj. The ruling is that a woman cannot travel for Hajj without a mahram or husband if the distance is three days or more. The proof is the hadith and fear of fitnah. Then it discusses the obligation of paying for the mahram's expenses. The preferred view (al-sahih) obligates her to pay, but al-Khujandi disagrees. The reconciliation: if he stipulates expenses, she must pay; otherwise not. The question of whether the mahram is a condition of obligation (shart wujub) or performance (shart ada) depends on the safety of the route — if safe, it is a condition of performance; if unsafe, it is a condition of obligation. This has practical implications: if she goes without mahram, is her Hajj valid? According to the text, it is valid but disliked, which indicates it is not a condition of obligation (since the obligation itself would not be valid if a condition is missing). The text also compares to the prohibition of khalwa.

Key Terms
محرم (mahram): A male relative whom a woman cannot marry permanently, such as father, brother, son, or husband.
شروط الوجوب (shurut al-wujub): Conditions that must be present for the Hajj to be obligatory upon a person.
شروط الأداء (shurut al-ada): Conditions that must be present for the performance of Hajj to be valid.

Hajj of a Minor and a Slave: Binding and Non-Binding Ihram
Ruling on minor and slave's Hajj after status change

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi ruling on the Hajj of a minor who attains puberty after ihram from that of a slave freed after ihram, and explain why the minor can renew his ihram but the slave cannot, based on the binding nature of their ihram.

Core Idea
A minor's ihram is non-binding and can be renewed; a slave's ihram is binding and cannot be converted.

This lesson examines the distinction between the Hajj of a minor and a slave when their status changes during the pilgrimage. While both initially perform nafl Hajj, the legal consequences differ sharply due to the binding nature of their ihram. Understanding this distinction reveals how the Hanafi school treats capacity and obligation.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling for both
Identify the Hanafi ruling: when a minor attains puberty after ihram or a slave is freed after ihram, their Hajj does not count as the obligatory Hajj if they continue without changing intention.
2
Investigate the reason: bindingness of ihram
The text explains that their ihram was contracted for nafl, so it cannot convert to fard. However, the key difference is that the minor's ihram is not binding (lazim) due to lack of legal capacity, while the slave's ihram is binding.
3
Analyze the renewal exception for the minor
The minor can renew his ihram before the standing at Arafah with the intention of Hajj al-Islam, and it becomes valid as obligatory. The slave cannot do this because his initial ihram is binding and cannot be annulled.
4
Compare consequences for penalties and qada
If the minor breaks his ihram, no qada or fidyah is required. For the slave, qada and fidyah are required because his ihram is binding and he has full legal capacity for penalties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا بلغ الصبي بعد ما أحرم أو أعتق العبد، فمضيا على ذلك : لم يُجْزِهما عن حَجَّةَ الإسلام .
* قوله: (وإذا بلغ الصبي بعد ما أحرم، أو أعتق العبد، فمضيا على حجهما ذلك : لم يُجْزِهما عن حجة الإسلام).
لأن إحرامهما انعقد لأداء النفل ، فلا ينقلب لأداء الفرض.
فإن جدد الصبيُّ الإحرام قبل الوقوف، فنوى حجة الإسلام: جاز. والعبد لو فعل ذلك: لم يجز ؛ لأن إحرام الصبي غير لازم ؛ لعدم الأهلية، ولهذا لو أحصر، فتحلل : لا يلزمه القضاء، وإن تناول شيئاً من
محظورات الإحرام لا يلزمه الجزاء، والعبد: يلزمه القضاء والجزاء. فإذا جدد الصبي: ينفسخ الأول بالثاني، والعبد إذا جدد: لا ينفسخ الأول، فلا ينعقد الثاني.
ولأن إحرام العبد لازم، فلا يمكنه الخروج عنه.
وإذا حج الفقير أجزأه عن حجة الإسلام، حتى لو استغنى بعد ذلك: لا تلزمه حجةٌ أخرى؛ لأن اشتراط الزاد والراحلة في حقه؛ للتيسير، لا لإثبات أهلية الوجوب، فكان سقوط الحج عنه : نظير سقوط أداء الصوم، وصلاة الجمعة عن المسافر.
ولهذا يجب الحج على الفقير بمكة، ولا يجب على العبيد بها؛ لأنهم ليسوا من أهل الوجوب.
And when a minor attains puberty after he has assumed ihram, or a slave is freed, and they continue upon that, it does not suffice them for the Hajj of Islam. His statement: (And when a minor attains puberty after he has assumed ihram, or a slave is freed, and they continue upon their hajj, it does not suffice them for the Hajj of Islam). Because their ihram was contracted for performing supererogatory worship, so it cannot be converted to obligatory. So if the minor renews ihram before standing (at Arafat), intending the Hajj of Islam, it is permissible. But if the slave does that, it is not permissible; because the minor's ihram is not binding due to lack of legal capacity, and therefore if he is prevented and disengages, no makeup is required, and if he commits any of the prohibitions of ihram, no penalty is required; whereas the slave is bound by makeup and penalties. So when the minor renews, the first is annulled by the second; but when the slave renews, the first is not annulled, so the second does not take effect. And because the ihram of the slave is binding, so he cannot exit from it. And when a poor person performs Hajj, it suffices him for the Hajj of Islam, even if he becomes wealthy afterwards: no other Hajj is required of him; because the condition of provisions and mount for him is for facilitation, not to establish capacity of obligation. Thus his falling of Hajj is analogous to the falling of performing fast and Friday prayer for a traveler. Therefore, Hajj is obligatory upon the poor person in Makkah, but not upon slaves there, because they are not among those who are obligated.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage covers two distinct rulings. First, if a minor (who has not reached puberty) assumes ihram and then reaches puberty during Hajj, or a slave is freed during Hajj, their Hajj does not count as the obligatory Hajj of Islam because their initial ihram was for supererogatory (nafl) worship, and it cannot transform into fard. However, the minor has an exception: if he renews his ihram before the standing at Arafah with the intention of Hajj al-Islam, it is valid. The slave cannot do this because his ihram is binding (lazim), whereas the minor's is not binding due to lack of legal capacity (ahliyyah). The difference arises because a minor cannot be held liable for penalties or compensation, but a slave is fully liable. The second ruling: a poor person (faqir) who performs Hajj fulfills his obligation, even if he later becomes wealthy. This is because the requirement of provision and mount (zad and rahilah) is for his convenience (taysir), not to establish actual capacity. Thus, if a poor person in Makkah is able to perform Hajj, it is obligatory on him, but not on slaves there because slaves are not from the people of obligation (ahl al-wujub).

Key Terms
حجة الإسلام (hajjat al-islam): The obligatory Hajj that every Muslim must perform once in a lifetime if capable.
نفل (nafl): Supererogatory worship; any act of worship beyond what is obligatory.
لازم (lazim): Binding; an obligation that cannot be annulled without renewal.
أهلية (ahliyyah): Legal capacity; the eligibility to assume obligations and liabilities.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the minor's first ihram is not binding, while the slave's is binding.
B) Because the minor is not obligated to perform Hajj, but the slave is.
C) Because the slave's first ihram was for fard, not nafl.
D) Because the minor has not yet committed any prohibitions.

Key Takeaway
A minor's ihram is non-binding and can be annulled by a new ihram; a slave's ihram is binding and cannot be annulled, so the minor can make his Hajj count as obligatory by renewing, while the slave cannot.

Hajj of a Poor Person: Fulfillment of Obligation
Ruling on poor person's Hajj fulfilling obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a poor person's Hajj suffices for his obligatory Hajj even if he becomes wealthy later, using the principle that the condition of travel is for taysir (facilitation) not for establishing capacity.

Core Idea
A poor person's Hajj fulfills the obligation because the requirement of provision is for facilitation, not to establish capacity.

This lesson addresses the situation of a poor person (faqir) who performs Hajj without initially being obligated. The Hanafi school rules that his Hajj counts as his obligatory Hajj, even if he later becomes wealthy. This is based on a qiyas (analogy) to the traveler's exemption from fasting and Friday prayer.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling for the poor person
The text states: 'إذا حج الفقير أجزأه عن حجة الإسلام' – if a poor person performs Hajj, it suffices him for the Hajj of Islam.
2
Identify the apparent objection
One might think that since the poor person was not obligated to perform Hajj due to lack of wealth, his Hajj should not count as the obligatory one, especially if he later becomes wealthy.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The condition of possession of provisions and mount (zad and rahilah) is for the facilitation (taysir) of the journey, not to establish the capacity of obligation (ahliyyat al-wujub). Therefore, performing Hajj without these means does not negate the validity of the act.
4
Apply the analogy to the traveler
The text draws a parallel: just as a traveler is exempted from fasting and Friday prayer due to hardship, but if he performs them, they are valid and count, similarly, the poor person's Hajj counts.
The illa is that the exemption is for convenience, not incapacity.
5
Note the distinction for slaves
The text contrasts: Hajj is obligatory on the poor person in Makkah (because he can perform it without travel), but not on slaves in Makkah because slaves are not among the people of obligation (ahl al-wujub).

Hajj of Minor and Slave: Nafl or Fard?
Ruling on Hajj of minor/slave and poor person

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that the Hajj of a minor before puberty or a slave before freedom does not suffice for the obligatory Hajj, and explain why a minor can renew ihram but a slave cannot.

Core Idea
Hajj performed without full obligation is nafl; only the minor can renew intent, the slave's binding ihram prevents conversion.

This passage from Al-Jawhara al-Nayyira addresses a subtle fiqh scenario: what happens when a child enters ihram and then reaches puberty, or a slave is freed while in ihram? The answer hinges on the nature of obligation and the binding force of ihram. The text also extends the discussion to a poor person who performs Hajj, clarifying that the condition of means is for ease, not for establishing eligibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
State the ruling: if a minor reaches puberty after ihram, or a slave is freed after ihram, and they continue that Hajj, it does not count as the obligatory Hajj (Hajjat al-Islam).
2
Understand the rationale
The text gives the reason: their ihram was contracted for a supererogatory (nafl) act, and it cannot turn into an obligatory act. Recognise the principle that an act's type is determined at inception.
3
Distinguish between minor and slave
The minor can renew ihram before standing at Arafat with the intention of fard, and it is valid. The slave cannot. Explore why: the minor's ihram is not binding (lazim) due to lack of legal capacity; the slave's ihram is binding because the slave has legal capacity (though limited in other matters).
Evidence: minor's ihram is non-binding – if he is prevented (ihsar), no qada or penalties are due. Slave's ihram is binding – qada and penalties apply.
4
Apply the analogy to the poor person
The text adds that if a poor person performs Hajj despite lacking provisions and mount, it suffices for the obligatory Hajj. Even if he becomes wealthy later, no repeat is needed. This shows that the condition of means is for convenience, not for eligibility.
This is analogous to the traveler being exempted from fasting and Friday prayer – the obligation is dropped due to hardship, but if performed, it counts.
5
Identify the thamarah (practical consequence)
The practical difference: a minor who performed Hajj before puberty must perform another Hajj after puberty if he wishes to fulfill the obligation, unless he renewed ihram. A slave who performed Hajj before freedom must perform another Hajj after freedom; renewal is not an option.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا بلغ الصبي بعد ما أحرم أو أعتق العبد، فمضيا على ذلك : لم يُجْزِهما عن حَجَّةَ الإسلام .
* قوله: (وإذا بلغ الصبي بعد ما أحرم، أو أعتق العبد، فمضيا على حجهما ذلك : لم يُجْزِهما عن حجة الإسلام).
لأن إحرامهما انعقد لأداء النفل ، فلا ينقلب لأداء الفرض.
فإن جدد الصبيُّ الإحرام قبل الوقوف، فنوى حجة الإسلام: جاز. والعبد لو فعل ذلك: لم يجز ؛ لأن إحرام الصبي غير لازم ؛ لعدم الأهلية، ولهذا لو أحصر، فتحلل : لا يلزمه القضاء، وإن تناول شيئاً من محظورات الإحرام لا يلزمه الجزاء، والعبد: يلزمه القضاء والجزاء. فإذا جدد الصبي: ينفسخ الأول بالثاني، والعبد إذا جدد: لا ينفسخ الأول، فلا ينعقد الثاني.
ولأن إحرام العبد لازم، فلا يمكنه الخروج عنه.
وإذا حج الفقير أجزأه عن حجة الإسلام، حتى لو استغنى بعد ذلك: لا تلزمه حجةٌ أخرى؛ لأن اشتراط الزاد والراحلة في حقه؛ للتيسير، لا لإثبات أهلية الوجوب، فكان سقوط الحج عنه : نظير سقوط أداء الصوم، وصلاة الجمعة عن المسافر.
ولهذا يجب الحج على الفقير بمكة، ولا يجب على العبيد بها؛ لأنهم ليسوا من أهل الوجوب.
And if a minor attains puberty after having entered ihram, or a slave is freed, and they continue upon that (their ihram and Hajj), it does not suffice them for the Hajj of Islam. * His statement: (And if a minor attains puberty after having entered ihram, or a slave is freed, and they continue upon that (their ihram and Hajj), it does not suffice them for the Hajj of Islam). Because their ihram was contracted for performing a supererogatory (act), so it cannot turn into performing an obligatory (act). Then if the minor renews ihram before standing (at Arafat) intending the Hajj of Islam, it is permissible. But if the slave does that, it is not permissible; because the minor's ihram is not binding due to lack of legal capacity, and for this reason if he is prevented (from completing Hajj) and exits ihram, qada is not incumbent upon him, and if he consumes something from the prohibitions of ihram, compensation is not incumbent upon him. But the slave: qada and compensation are incumbent upon him. So when the minor renews (ihram), the first is dissolved by the second. And if the slave renews (ihram), the first is not dissolved, so the second does not take effect. And because the slave's ihram is binding, so he cannot exit it. And if a poor person performs Hajj, it suffices him for the Hajj of Islam, even if he becomes wealthy afterward, another Hajj is not incumbent upon him; because the condition of provisions and mount in his case is for facility, not to establish eligibility for obligation. So the dropping of Hajj from him is analogous to the dropping of performing fast and Friday prayer from the traveler. And for this reason, Hajj is obligatory upon the poor person in Makkah, but not upon slaves there, because they are not among the people of obligation.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from Al-Jawhara al-Nayyira, explaining the matn of Al-Quduri on the Hajj of a minor and a slave. The ruling states that if a child enters ihram and then reaches puberty before completing Hajj, or a slave is freed while in ihram, and they continue with the same Hajj, it does not count as their obligatory Hajj (Hajjat al-Islam). The reason given is that their ihram was initially contracted for a supererogatory (nafl) act, and it is not possible to convert it into an obligatory act retroactively. A key distinction is made between a minor and a slave: the minor can cancel his first ihram and renew it with the intention of obligatory Hajj before standing at Arafat, and this is valid because a minor's ihram is not legally binding (ghayr lazim) due to his incomplete legal capacity. For example, if a minor is prevented from completing Hajj (ihsar), he does not have to make it up (qada), and if he violates prohibitions of ihram, no penalty is due. In contrast, a slave's ihram is binding (lazim); he must make up if prevented and owes penalties for violations. Therefore, if a slave tries to renew ihram, the first remains intact, and the new ihram does not take effect. The passage also extends the discussion to a poor person: if a poor person performs Hajj despite lacking the means, it counts as his obligatory Hajj, and even if he later becomes wealthy, no repeat is required. This is because the requirement of provisions and mount is for convenience, not to establish eligibility. Similarly, a poor person residing in Makkah must perform Hajj, but a slave there is not obliged because slaves lack the capacity for obligation.

Key Terms
إحرام (ihram): The state of consecration for Hajj or Umrah, entered by intention and talbiyah.
نفل (nafl): A supererogatory act of worship, not obligatory.
لازم (lazim): Binding; an ihram that cannot be exited without completing the rituals.
حجة الإسلام (hajjat al-islam): The obligatory Hajj that every free, adult Muslim must perform once in a lifetime.
أهلية (ahliyyah): Legal capacity; the ability to be subject to obligations and rights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does a slave's renewal of ihram not take effect according to the Hanafi ruling?
A) Because a slave cannot intend Hajj at all.
B) Because the slave's first ihram is binding (lazim) and cannot be dissolved.
C) Because the slave must obtain his master's permission first.
D) Because the slave's ihram was for nafl and cannot be changed.

Key Takeaway
Hajj performed before full legal capacity (minority or slavery) is valid as nafl but does not fulfill the fard obligation; only a minor can convert it by renewing ihram with fard intention before standing at Arafat.

The Five Miqat (Places for Ihram)
Lists the five miqat and explains their purpose

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five miqat, identify their corresponding regions, and explain when crossing them requires ihram.

Core Idea
Five fixed miqat: Dhu al-Hulayfah, Dhat 'Irq, al-Juhfah, Qarn al-Manazil, Yalamlam.

This lesson presents the five places where a pilgrim must enter ihram before proceeding to Makkah. These miqat are established by the Prophet ﷺ and are agreed upon by all schools. Understanding them is essential for valid Hajj or Umrah. The text also clarifies a key nuance: the restriction applies only when heading to the Haram, not to the general areas outside Makkah.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the five miqat and their regions
Learn the name of each miqat and the region it serves: Dhu al-Hulayfah (Medina), Dhat 'Irq (Iraq), al-Juhfah (Syria), Qarn al-Manazil (Najd), Yalamlam (Yemen). Use the poetic couplet as a mnemonic.
The correct pronunciation of Qarn is with sukun on the ra (Qarn).
2
Understand the scope of the prohibition
The text states that it is not permissible to pass these miqat except as a muhrim. The commentary specifies that this prohibition is only for those intending to enter Makkah. If one is going to the hill (the area outside the Haram but within the miqat region), it is permissible to pass without ihram.
3
Apply the ruling to a modern scenario
Imagine a person from Najd traveling to Jeddah (in the hill) for business. Since Jeddah is not Makkah, they may pass Qarn al-Manazil without ihram. However, if the same person later decides to go to Makkah for Umrah, they must exit Jeddah to the nearest miqat (or return to Qarn) to enter ihram.
This is a common point of confusion.

And the miqat (places) which it is not permissible for a person to pass except as muhrim are five: For the people of Medina: Dhu al-Hulayfah. For the people of Iraq: Dhat 'Irq. For the people of Syria: al-Juhfah. For the people of Najd: Qarn al-Manazil. For the people of Yemen: Yalamlam. [Heading: The spatial miqat for Hajj] * His statement: (And the miqat which it is not permissible for a person to pass except as muhrim are five), meaning: he should not pass them towards Makkah. As for going to the Hill (outside the Haram), it is permissible without ihram. * His statement: (For the people of Medina: Dhu al-Hulayfah, for the people of Iraq: Dhat 'Irq, for the people of Syria: al-Juhfah, for the people of Najd: Qarn with sukun on the ra, that is the correct [pronunciation], as in 'Shams al-'Ulum'. His statement: (And for the people of Yemen: Yalamlam). And someone composed two couplets about them, saying: 'Irq al-Iraq, Yalamlam al-Yamani, and at Dhu al-Hulayfah the Madani enters ihram. For Sham, al-Juhfah if you pass by it, and for the people of Najd, Qarn, so take it.'
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling on the five spatial miqat (mawaqit al-hajjiyyah) for entering the state of ihram. It states that there are five places that a person intending to perform Hajj or Umrah may not cross without being in ihram. The miqat are: Dhu al-Hulayfah (for the people of Medina), Dhat 'Irq (for Iraqis), al-Juhfah (for Syrians), Qarn al-Manazil (for Najd), and Yalamlam (for Yemen). The commentary explains that these restrictions apply only when one intends to enter Makkah; if one is going to the outer areas (al-hill, the area outside the Haram but still within the miqat region), it is permissible to pass without ihram. This is an important nuance: the miqat are checkpoints for those heading to the Haram itself. The passage also notes the correct pronunciation of 'Qarn' with sukun on the ra, and includes a poetic couplet for memorization. This is a foundational ruling in the Hanafi school, based on authentic hadith, and is agreed upon by all schools as the basic rule, though there may be slight differences in exact locations.

Key Terms
مواقيت (mawaqit): Places designated for entering the state of ihram for Hajj or Umrah.
مُحْرِم (muhrim): A person who has entered the state of ihram, intending Hajj or Umrah.
حِلّ (hill): The area outside the Haram (sanctuary) of Makkah but within the miqat region.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The five miqat are fixed checkpoints for entering ihram when heading to Makkah; crossing them without ihram is haram and requires a dam.

The Five Miqats and Rules of Crossing
Defines the five miqats and explains the rules for crossing them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five miqats, apply the rule that one must enter ihram before passing them when heading to Mecca, and identify the exceptions for residents within the miqat and travelers by sea.

Core Idea
Passing the miqat without ihram toward Mecca incurs a dam; concessions exist for frequent travel and hardship.

The miqats are fixed boundaries set by the Prophet ﷺ for entering the state of ihram. This lesson clarifies the five miqats and the nuanced rules: the obligation applies only when heading to Mecca, not elsewhere, and residents living between the miqat and Mecca have a concession to enter without ihram for ordinary needs. Understanding these details prevents unnecessary hardship and ensures correct practice for Hajj and Umrah.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the five miqats and their assigned regions
Learn the names: Dhu al-Hulayfah (Medina), Dhat 'Irq (Iraq), al-Juhfah (Syria), Qarn al-Manazil (Najd), Yalamlam (Yemen). Note the poetry that helps retain them.
2
Grasp the general obligation: ihram required when heading to Mecca
The text states: 'لا يجوز أن يتجاوزها الإنسان إلا محرماً' – one must not pass these miqats except in ihram when the destination is Mecca. If heading to the Hill (area outside Haram but inside miqat boundaries), ihram is not needed.
3
Identify the exception for residents within the miqat (hill)
Those whose homes are between the miqat and Mecca may enter Mecca without ihram for non-pilgrimage needs due to frequent entry and manifest hardship (حرج). Only when they intend Hajj or Umrah must they enter ihram.
This is a specific Hanafi leniency based on the principle of raf' al-haraj.
4
Apply rules for travelers by sea and unmarked land routes
For sea travel, the miqat is when the ship aligns with a corresponding land miqat. For unmarked land routes, one enters ihram when aligning with any of the five miqats. For Egyptians, the miqat is the alignment of al-Juhfah.
5
Handle cases of missing the miqat and entering at another
If one passes his own miqat without ihram and then reaches another miqat and enters ihram there, it is valid, but it is better to enter at the first miqat. A dam (sacrifice) may be required for passing without ihram.
The text does not detail the dam, but it is known from other sources.

Source Text

ولأهل العراق : ذات عرق .
ولأهل الشام : الجُحْفَةُ .
ولأهل نَجْدِ : قَرْنُ المنازل.
ولأهل اليمن : يَلَمْلَمُ.
مواقيت الحج المكانية :

يعني لا يتجاوزها إلى مكة.

من هذه المواقيت.

ومن جاوز ميقاته غير محرم، ثم أتى ميقاتاً آخر، فأحرم منه: أجزاء، إلا أن إحرامه من ميقاته أفضل.

بخلاف ما إذا أرادوا النُّسَك : فإنه لا يباح لهم دخولها إلا بالإحرام؛ لأنه يتفق أحياناً، فلا حرج.
The Book of Hajj. The miqats (stations) which a person is not allowed to pass except in a state of ihram are five: For the people of Medina: Dhu al-Hulayfah. For the people of Iraq: Dhat 'Irq. For the people of Syria: al-Juhfah. For the people of Najd: Qarn al-Manazil. For the people of Yemen: Yalamlam. (Commentary) His saying: "The miqats which a person is not allowed to pass except in a state of ihram are five" – meaning he does not pass them going towards Mecca. As for going towards the Hill (area outside the Haram), it is permitted without ihram. His saying: "For the people of Medina: Dhu al-Hulayfah, for the people of Iraq: Dhat 'Irq, for the people of Syria: al-Juhfah, for the people of Najd: Qarn (with sukun over the ra, that is the correct form) – as in Shams al-'Ulum. His saying: "For the people of Yemen: Yalamlam." And some of them set it into two lines of poetry, saying: [Arabic poetry]. (Then the matn continues) If one performs ihram before these miqats, it is permissible. And if one's home is after the miqats (i.e., between them and Mecca), then his miqat is the Hill (the area outside the Haram). And if one performs Hajj by sea, his miqat is when he aligns with a point on land, and he must not pass it except in ihram. Similarly, if he travels by land via an unmarked route, he should enter ihram when he aligns with one of these miqats. For the people of Egypt, their miqat is the alignment of al-Juhfah. And if one passes his miqat without ihram, then reaches another miqat and performs ihram from there, it suffices, except that performing ihram from his own miqat is better. (Commentary) His saying: "If one performs ihram before these miqats, it is permissible" – and it is better if he feels safe from committing prohibited acts; otherwise, delaying to the miqat is better. His saying: "And if one's home is after the miqats, then his miqat is the Hill" – meaning in Hajj and Umrah. And it is permissible for them to enter Mecca without ihram if it is for a need, because they frequently enter Mecca, and requiring ihram each time involves manifest hardship. Unlike when they intend a pilgrimage (nusk), then it is not allowed for them to enter except with ihram, because it happens only occasionally, so no hardship.
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the matn of al-Quduri, with commentary by the author of al-Jawharah al-Nayyirah. It defines the five miqats (stations for entering ihram) for Hajj and Umrah, and explains the rules related to them. The five miqats are: Dhu al-Hulayfah for the people of Medina, Dhat 'Irq for Iraq, al-Juhfah for Syria, Qarn al-Manazil for Najd, and Yalamlam for Yemen. The commentator notes that one must not pass these stations except in a state of ihram when heading towards Mecca. However, if one is heading to the Hill (area outside the Haram), ihram is not required. The passage also addresses several scenarios: (1) If one performs ihram before reaching the miqat, it is valid, and it is actually better if one is confident of avoiding prohibited acts; otherwise, delaying to the miqat is better. (2) If a person's home lies between the miqat and Mecca (i.e., within the miqat area), then their miqat is the Hill (the area outside the Haram but before the Haram boundaries). This is because they frequently enter Mecca, and requiring ihram for every entry would cause hardship. However, if they intend a pilgrimage (Hajj or Umrah), ihram is required. (3) For those traveling by sea, their miqat is when the ship aligns with a corresponding land miqat. Similarly, if traveling by an unmarked land route, one should enter ihram when aligning with one of the five miqats. (4) For the people of Egypt, their miqat is the alignment of al-Juhfah. (5) If someone passes their miqat without ihram, then reaches another miqat and enters ihram there, it is valid, but it is better to enter ihram at the first miqat. The passage emphasizes the principle of avoiding hardship (raf' al-haraj), as seen in the concession for residents within the miqat to enter Mecca without ihram for ordinary needs.

Key Terms
ميقات (miqat): A designated place and time for entering ihram; here specifically the place (miqat makani) beyond which one may not pass without ihram.
ذو الحليفة (Dhu al-Hulayfah): The miqat for people of Medina, located about 6 miles from Medina.
ذات عرق (Dhat 'Irq): The miqat for people of Iraq, located in the desert.
الجحفة (al-Juhfah): The miqat for people of Syria (and Egypt by alignment), near Rabigh.
قرن المنازل (Qarn al-Manazil): The miqat for people of Najd, also known as al-Sayl al-Kabir.
يلملم (Yalamlam): The miqat for people of Yemen, a mountain pass.
إحرام (ihram): The state of consecration for Hajj or Umrah, entered by intention and talbiyah, and wearing specific garments.
حل (hill (al-hill)): The area outside the Haram (sanctuary) of Mecca, but within the miqat boundaries.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The five miqats are fixed places; entering ihram before crossing them when heading to Mecca is wajib, but concessions exist for residents within the miqat and for those not intending pilgrimage.

Permissibility of Early Ihram Before the Miqat
States permissibility of ihram before miqat and preference condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on entering ihram before the miqat and identify the condition that makes it preferable or better to delay.

Core Idea
Early ihram is permissible and even preferred if safe from prohibitions; otherwise delay.

The miqats are fixed boundaries set by the Prophet (ﷺ) for entering ihram. A common question is whether one may enter ihram before reaching these boundaries. This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on early ihram, which balances permissibility with practical considerations of avoiding the prohibitions of ihram.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is entering ihram before reaching the designated miqat boundary. The basic ruling is 'jaza' – it is permissible (ja'iz).
2
Note the preference condition
The commentator adds that early ihram is better (afdal) if one is safe from committing the prohibitions of ihram (muharramat).
Muharramat include wearing perfume, shaving, cutting nails, etc.
3
Identify the alternative scenario
If one fears falling into any prohibited act, then delaying ihram until the miqat is better.
This balances the virtue of early consecration with avoiding sin.
4
Understand the practical implication
The ruling is conditional: early ihram is recommended only when the pilgrim can confidently avoid prohibitions for the extra time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قدَّم الإحرام على هذه المواقيت : جاز. قوله: (فإن قدَّم الإحرام على هذه المواقيت: جاز)، وهو الأفضل إذا أمن من مواقعة المحظورات، وإلا فالتأخير إلى الميقات أفضل.
If he brings forward the ihram before these miqats: it is permissible. His statement: 'If he brings forward the ihram before these miqats: it is permissible', and it is better if he is safe from committing prohibited things, otherwise delaying until the miqat is better.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with expansion. It first states the basic ruling: entering ihram before reaching the designated miqat boundaries is legally permissible (ja'iz). The commentator then clarifies that in fact it is preferable (afdal) to enter ihram early if one is confident of avoiding the prohibitions of ihram (such as shaving, wearing perfume, etc.) during the extra time. However, if one fears falling into any prohibited act, then it is better to delay ihram until reaching the miqat. This balanced ruling reflects the Hanafi principle of considering potential hardship and the act's recommended nature.

Key Terms
المَواقيت (al-mawaqit): The fixed places/boundaries designated for entering ihram for Hajj and Umrah; e.g., Dhu'l-Hulayfah, al-Juhfah, Qarn al-Manazil, etc.
الإحرام (al-ihram): The state of ritual consecration for Hajj/Umrah, marked by intention and talbiyah, and entry into prohibitions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on entering ihram before reaching the miqat?
A) It is invalid and must be repeated from the miqat.
B) It is permissible but makruh.
C) It is permissible and even better if one can avoid the prohibitions of ihram.
D) It is obligatory to enter ihram exactly at the miqat.

Key Takeaway
Entering ihram before the miqat is permissible and even preferred if one can avoid the prohibitions; otherwise, delay is better.

The Miqat and Entry Concession for Residents Beyond the Miqat
Explains the miqat and special concession for residents beyond the miqat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the miqat for residents beyond the miqat and apply the ruling for entering Makkah with or without ihram based on the intention (need vs. nusuk).

Core Idea
Residents beyond the miqat may enter Makkah without ihram for need, but must do so for pilgrimage.

This passage clarifies the special status of those who live between the miqat and Makkah. While their miqat for Hajj and Umrah is al-hill, they are granted flexibility for frequent non-pilgrimage entries to avoid hardship. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying the rules of ihram.

Guided Path
1
Identify the category
Who is being discussed? Those whose permanent residence lies after the miqat (i.e., between the miqat and the Haram).
2
State the miqat for rituals
For both Hajj and Umrah, their miqat is al-hill (the open land outside the Haram).
Al-hill is not a fixed point but the area beyond the Haram boundaries.
3
Distinguish between need and nusuk
If entering Makkah for a mundane need (shopping, visit), they may do so without ihram. If intending the rituals (nusuk), they must enter with ihram.
The key is the intention (niyyah).
4
Understand the rationale
The concession is based on removing hardship (raf‘ al-haraj): frequent travel makes ihram every time burdensome. Rituals are occasional, so no hardship.
This is a Hanafi application of the maxim ‘al-mashaqqah tajlib al-taysir’.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومَن كان منزله بعد المواقيت : فميقاتُه الحِلَّ . قوله: (ومن كان منزله بعد المواقيت: فوقته الحل)، يعني في الحج والعمرة. ويجوز لهم دخول مكة بغير إحرام إذا كان لحاجة؛ لأنه يكثر دخول مكة، وفي إيجاب الإحرام في كل دخلة: حرج ظاهر. بخلاف ما إذا أرادوا النُّسَك : فإنه لا يباح لهم دخولها إلا بالإحرام؛ لأنه يتفق أحياناً، فلا حرج.
And whoever's house is after the miqats: his miqat is the hill. His statement: 'And whoever's house is after the miqats: his time is the hill', meaning in Hajj and Umrah. And it is permissible for them to enter Makkah without ihram if there is a need, because they frequently enter Makkah, and in making ihram obligatory every time there is manifest hardship. Contrary to when they intend the rituals: then it is not permitted for them to enter except with ihram, because it happens occasionally, so no hardship.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with a reasoned proof based on the principle of removing hardship (raf' al-haraj). It addresses residents whose homes are situated between the miqat and the Haram (i.e., beyond the miqat towards Makkah). Their miqat for both Hajj and Umrah is the Hill (al-hill), meaning the open land outside the Haram boundaries. The commentator then introduces a significant concession: such residents are permitted to enter Makkah without ihram for mundane needs (like shopping, visiting) due to the frequency of their visits. Requiring ihram every time would cause manifest hardship (haraj). However, when they intend to perform the pilgrimage rituals (Hajj or Umrah), they must enter Makkah in a state of ihram, as these occasions are infrequent and do not cause hardship. This distinction is a practical application of the Hanafi legal maxim 'al-mashaqqah tajlib al-taysir' (hardship brings ease).

Key Terms
الحِل (al-hill): The area outside the Haram (sacred territory) but before the miqat boundaries; the open land.
النسُك (an-nusuk): The rituals of Hajj or Umrah; pilgrimage acts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
For residents beyond the miqat, entering Makkah without ihram is permissible for needs but obligatory for pilgrimage, based on the principle of hardship removal.

Miqat for Makkah Residents: Haram for Hajj, Hill for Umrah
States miqat for Makkan residents for Hajj and Umrah and consequence of error.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the miqat rules for a Makkan resident and apply the consequence of violating them.

Core Idea
A Makkan's miqat: for Hajj from the Haram, for Umrah from the Hill; error requires a dam.

Makkah residents have distinct miqat for Hajj and Umrah. The rule is not arbitrary: it ensures each pilgrimage involves a journey (safar) from one zone to the other. The commentator explains the reasoning (ta'lil) and then gives the best practice and penalty for mistakes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two miqat rules
From the text: for Hajj, miqat is al-Haram (sacred territory). For Umrah, miqat is al-Hill (outside Haram).
2
Understand the ta'lil (reasoning)
The text reasons: Hajj's essential act (standing at Arafat) is in the Hill, so ihram from Haram creates a journey. Umrah's acts are in Haram, so ihram from Hill creates a journey into Haram.
This shows fiqh is based on cause (safar).
3
Note the best practice for Umrah miqat
The best place for Makkans to enter ihram for Umrah is at-Tan'im. The text gives an etymology of its name.
at-Tan'im is on the boundary of the Haram.
4
Apply the consequence of error
If a Makkan performs ihram for Hajj from the Hill or for Umrah from the Haram (swapping miqat), he must offer a dam (sacrificial animal) as compensation.
The Hajj/Umrah remains valid but wajib is missed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومَن كان بمكة : فميقاته في الحج : الحرم، وفي العمرة : الحِلُّ. قوله: (ومن كان بمكة فميقاتُه في الحج: الحرم، وفي العمرة: الحل؛ لأن أداء الحج في عرفة، وعرفةُ في الحل، فيكون الإحرام من الحرم؛ ليتحقق نوع سفر، وهو من الحرم إلى الحل. وأداء العمرة في الحرم، وهو الطواف والسعي، فيكون الإحرام لها من الحل؛ ليتحقق نوع سفر من الحرم إلى الحل، وأداء العمرة في الحرم، وهو الطواف والسعي، فيكون الإحرام لها من الحل؛ ليتحقق نوع سفر، وهو الإحرام من الحل إلى الحرم. والأفضل: من التنعيم. وإنما سمي التنعيم؛ لأن عن يمينه جبلاً يسمى: نعيم، وعن يساره جبلا يسمى: ناعم، والوادي نُعمان. ولو ترك المكي ميقاته، وأحرم للحج في الحل، وللعمرة في الحرم: يجب عليه دم.
And whoever is in Makkah: his miqat for Hajj is the Haram, and for Umrah is the Hill. His statement: 'And whoever is in Makkah, his miqat for Hajj is the Haram, and for Umrah is the Hill; because the performance of Hajj is at Arafat, and Arafat is in the Hill, so the ihram is from the Haram to achieve a type of journey, from the Haram to the Hill. And the performance of Umrah is in the Haram, which is tawaf and sa'i, so ihram for it is from the Hill to achieve a type of journey from the Haram to the Hill, and the performance of Umrah is in the Haram, tawaf and sa'i, so ihram for it is from the Hill to achieve a type of journey, which is ihram from the Hill to the Haram. And the best is from at-Tan'im. And it is named at-Tan'im because on its right is a mountain called Na'im, and on its left a mountain called Na'im, and the valley is Nu'man. And if the Makkan leaves his miqat and performs ihram for Hajj in the Hill and for Umrah in the Haram, he must offer a dam (sacrifice).
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof (ta'lil) and an etymology. It specifies the miqat for residents of Makkah: for Hajj, the miqat is within the Haram (the sacred territory), because the essential act of Hajj, standing at Arafat, takes place in the Hill (outside the Haram). Thus, ihram from the Haram ensures a journey (safar) from the Haram to the Hill, which is the intended type of travel. For Umrah, the miqat is the Hill, because the essential acts (tawaf and sa'i) are inside the Haram, ensuring a journey from the Hill into the Haram. The commentator advises that the best place for Umrah ihram is at-Tan'im, explaining its name from two mountains, Na'im and Na'im, and the valley Nu'man. Finally, the ruling on error: if a Makkan does ihram for Hajj from the Hill or for Umrah from the Haram (i.e., swaps the miqat), he must offer a dam (a sacrificial animal) as compensation. This highlights the strictness of miqat observance for Makkans.

Key Terms
الحرم (al-haram): The sacred territory of Makkah, boundaries defined by the Prophet (ﷺ), within which certain acts are restricted (e.g., hunting, cutting trees).
التنعيم (at-tan'im): A place on the boundary of the Haram near Makkah, used as a miqat for Umrah for Makkans; recommended by the Prophet (ﷺ).
دَم (dam): A sacrificial animal (sheep, goat, etc.) offered as compensation for a violation of a wajib act in Hajj/Umrah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) It is invalid and must be repeated.

D) He must perform the Hajj again next year.

Key Takeaway
Makkans must enter ihram for Hajj from the Haram and for Umrah from the Hill; violation requires dam.

Cleansing Before Ihram: Ghusl or Wudu
Describes the preferred cleansing before ihram and its nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on cleansing before ihram and explain why menstruating women are included.

Core Idea
Ghusl before ihram is for cleanliness, not ritual purity — so even menstruating women should perform it.

This passage from Al-Jawharah an-Nayyirah explains the recommended purification before entering ihram. The commentator clarifies a key Hanafi distinction: this ghusl is for nazafah (cleanliness), not taharah (ritual purity). This distinction has practical implications, especially for menstruating and postpartum women.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being described and its legal classification. The passage discusses ghusl or wudu before ihram, and states that ghusl is better.
The ruling is sunnah mu'akkadah, not wajib.
2
Distinguish between ghusl and wudu
Note the preference: ghusl (full wash) is afdal (better) than wudu, but either suffices.
3
Understand the reason (illah) for ghusl
The commentator explicitly says 'al-ghusl huna li-n-nazafah la li-t-taharah'. This means the purpose is physical cleanliness, not ritual purification.
This is a crucial Hanafi principle: nazafah vs taharah.
4
Apply the ruling to special cases
Because the ghusl is for cleanliness, it is recommended even for menstruating women (ha'id) and those in postpartum bleeding (nafsa'), who are not ritually pure.
No obligation, but recommended for cleanliness.
5
Note cross-madhhab status
Some other schools may not explicitly include menstruating women for this ghusl. The Hanafi school is unique in affirming it for them due to the nazafah purpose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا أراد الإحرام: اغتسل، أو توضأ والغُسلُ أفضل. قوله: (وإذا أراد الإحرام اغتسل أو توضأ، والغسل أفضل)؛ سواء أراد الإحرام بالحج أو بالعمرة أو بهما. والغُسل هنا : للنظافة، لا للطهارة، حتى إنه تُؤمر به الحائض والنفساء.
And when he intends ihram: he should take a bath or perform wudu, and taking a bath is better. His statement: 'And when he intends ihram, he takes a bath or performs wudu, and taking a bath is better'; whether he intends ihram for Hajj or Umrah or both. And the ghusl here is for cleanliness, not for purification, so much so that a menstruating woman and a woman in postpartum bleeding are commanded to do it.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement. It details the sunnah acts before entering ihram for Hajj or Umrah. The preferred option is to perform ghusl (full body wash), but wudu (ablution) suffices. This applies regardless of the type of pilgrimage (Hajj only, Umrah only, or both combined as qiran). The commentator makes an important distinction: this ghusl is for physical cleanliness (nazafah) and not for ritual purity (taharah). Therefore, even a menstruating woman (ha'id) or a woman with postpartum bleeding (nafsa') is instructed to perform this ghusl before ihram, as it is purely for cleanliness and not a condition for the validity of ihram. This ruling reflects the Hanafi understanding that the state of ihram does not require ritual purity; rather, cleanliness is merely recommended.

Key Terms
الغُسل (ghusl): Washing the entire body; a recommended act before ihram for cleanliness.
النظافة (nazafah): Physical cleanliness, as opposed to ritual purity (taharah). It does not require the removal of major or minor impurity.
الحائض والنفساء (al-ha'id wa an-nafsa'): A menstruating woman and a woman in post-partum bleeding; they are obliged to perform ghusl before ihram despite being ritually impure.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Miqat for Residents of Makkah: The Rationale and Ruling
Explains the miqat for Makkans and the wisdom behind it

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the miqat for a Makkan for Hajj and Umrah, explain the rationale behind the distinction, and identify the penalty for violation.

Core Idea
A Makkan's Hajj ihram begins in the Haram; Umrah ihram begins in the Hill because each act's venue determines the required direction of travel.

This passage clarifies a key difference in miqat for those already residing in Makkah. Unlike pilgrims from outside who approach from their respective miqats, a Makkan must consider the location of the main ritual acts. The author provides a logical rationale (talil) that explains why the Haram is the miqat for Hajj and the Hill for Umrah, and warns of the penalty for neglecting this boundary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios and their miqats
For Hajj, the miqat for a Makkan is the Haram (the sacred precinct itself). For Umrah, it is the Hill (the area outside the Haram boundaries).
This is the core ruling.
2
Locate the rationale (hikmah) for each miqat
Hajj's main act (standing at Arafat) occurs in the Hill, so ihram is from the Haram to create a journey from Haram to Hill. Umrah's main acts (Tawaf and Sa'y) are in the Haram, so ihram is from the Hill to create a journey from Hill to Haram.
The rationale is based on the location of the rites, ensuring a valid travel (safar) is achieved.
3
Understand the recommended location (Tan'eem) and its etymology
Tan'eem is the best place for Umrah ihram. Its name derives from mountains called Na'eem and Na'em on either side, and the valley Nu'man.
The recommendation is due to its proximity and convenience.
4
Note the penalty for violating the miqat
If a Makkan deliberately enters ihram for Hajj from the Hill or for Umrah from the Haram, a dam (sacrificial sheep or goat) is obligatory.
This shows the miqat is a wajib condition; violation requires compensation, not invalidation of the pilgrimage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن كان بمكة : فميقاته في الحج : الحرم، وفي العمرة : الحل؛ لأن أداء الحج في عرفة، وعرفة في الحل، فيكون الإحرام من الحرم؛ ليتحقق نوع سفر، وهو من الحرم إلى الحل. وأداء العمرة في الحرم، وهو الطواف والسعي، فيكون الإحرام لها من الحل؛ ليتحقق نوع سفر، وهو الإحرام من الحل إلى الحرم. والأفضل: من التنعيم. وإنما سمي التنعيم؛ لأن عن يمينه جبلا يسمى نعيم، وعن يساره جبلا يسمى ناعم، والوادي نعمان. ولو ترك المكي ميقاته، وأحرم للحج في الحل، وللعمرة في الحرم: يجب عليه دم.
And whoever is in Makkah, his miqat for Hajj is the Haram, and for Umrah is the Hill; because the performance of Hajj is at Arafat, and Arafat is in the Hill, so ihram is from the Haram to realize a type of journey, which is from the Haram to the Hill. And the performance of Umrah is in the Haram, namely the Tawaf and Sa'y, so ihram for it is from the Hill to realize a type of journey, which is ihram from the Hill to the Haram. And the best is from Tan'eem. It is called Tan'eem because to its right is a mountain called Na'eem, and to its left a mountain called Na'em, and the valley is Nu'man. And if a Makkan leaves his miqat and performs ihram for Hajj in the Hill, and for Umrah in the Haram, a dam (sacrificial animal) is obligatory upon him.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the ruling concerning the miqat for those residing in Makkah. The author clarifies that their miqat for Hajj is the Haram itself, while for Umrah it is the Hill (al-hill), i.e., the area outside the Haram boundaries. The wisdom (hikmah) behind this distinction is that the main act of Hajj, standing at Arafat, takes place in the Hill. Therefore, ihram from the Haram ensures a journey from the Haram to the Hill, which is considered a valid travel (safar). For Umrah, the main acts—Tawaf and Sa'y—are performed within the Haram, so ihram from the Hill ensures a journey from the Hill to the Haram. The best place for performing Umrah ihram is Tan'eem, a location in the Hill close to Makkah. The passage also provides an etymology of the name 'Tan'eem'. Finally, if a Makkan violates these miqat rules by entering ihram for Hajj from the Hill or for Umrah from the Haram, he is obliged to offer a dam (sacrificial animal) as a penalty. This ruling is derived from the principle that the miqat is a wajib boundary that must be observed, and deliberate violation incurs a compensatory sacrifice.

Key Terms
ميقات (miqat): The designated boundary or time for entering ihram; here it refers to the spatial boundary for pilgrims residing in Makkah.
الحرم (al-haram): The sacred precinct of Makkah, within which certain acts are restricted.
الحل (al-hill): The area outside the Haram but outside the miqat boundaries; the non-sacred territory.
دم (dam): A sacrificial animal (goat or sheep) required as a penalty for violations during Hajj or Umrah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The miqat for a Makkan is determined by the location of the main ritual: Hajj's Arafat is in the Hill, so ihram from Haram; Umrah's Tawaf/Sa'y in Haram, so ihram from Hill.
