Ghusl and Wudu before Ihram
Ruling on ghusl/wudu before ihram

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ghusl before ihram and explain why it applies even to menstruating women.

Core Idea
Ghusl before ihram is for cleanliness, not purification, so it is sunnah for everyone, including menstruating women.

Before entering the state of ihram, a person is recommended to perform ghusl or wudu. This lesson clarifies the ruling, its purpose, and the scope of obligation according to the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is performing ghusl or wudu before ihram. The ruling is that it is sunnah (recommended), with ghusl being superior to wudu.
2
Analyze the purpose of ghusl
The text states ghusl here is for cleanliness (nazafah), not for ritual purity (taharah). This means it is not a requirement of purity but a physical and spiritual preparation.
3
Apply the ruling to special cases
Because it is for cleanliness, even a menstruating or postpartum woman is commanded to perform it, even though she is in a state of ritual impurity.
This is unique to the Hanafi school; some other schools may not recommend ghusl for menstruating women in this context.
4
Distinguish between ghusl and wudu
Both are valid, but ghusl is better. Wudu suffices if performing ghusl is difficult. The choice depends on the individual's ability and preference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما يفعله من أراد الإحرام :] قوله: (وإذا أراد الإحرام اغتسل أو توضأ، والغسل أفضل)؛ سواء أراد الإحرام بالحج أو بالعمرة أو بهما. والغسل هنا : للنظافة، لا للطهارة، حتى إنه تُؤمر به الحائض والنفساء. ولبس ثوبين جديدين، أو غَسِيلَيْن : إزاراً، ورداء . ومَسَ طِيْباً إِنْ كان له طِيْبٌ . وسمي الإحرام؛ لأنه يُحرِّم المباحات قبله من الطيب، ولبس المخيط، وغير ذلك. قوله: (ولبس ثوبين جديدين، أو غَسِيْلَين)، والجديد أفضل؛ لأنه أقرب إلى الطهارة من الآثام، ولهذا قدمه الشيخ على الغسيل. وإن لبس ثوباً واحداً أجزأه ؛ لأن المقصود ستر العورة من غير المخيط، وإنما ذكر ثوبين ؛ لأن المُحرِم ممنوع من لبس المخيط، ولا بد له من ستر العورة، ودفع الحر والبرد، وذلك إنما يحصل بالإزار والرداء. * قوله : ومَسَّ طِيْباً إن كان له طيب). هذا يدل على أن الطّيب من سنن الزوائد، وليس من سنن الهدى. - ولا يضرُّ أثر الطيب بعد الإحرام. وعن محمد: يكره أن يتطيب بما تبقى عينه بعد الإحرام. قلنا : ابتداء الطيب حصل من وجه مباح، فالبقاء عليه لا يضره، كالحلق، ولأن الممنوع منه : التطيب بعد الإحرام. ومحمد يقول: للبقاء حكم الابتداء، كما في لبس القميص إذا لبسه قبل الإحرام، ولم يخلعه بعده.
What the one who intends ihram does: His saying: 'When he intends ihram, he performs ghusl or wudu, and ghusl is better.' This applies whether he intends ihram for Hajj, Umrah, or both. Ghusl here is for cleanliness, not for ritual purification, so even a menstruating or postpartum woman is commanded to do it. And he wears two new garments, or washed ones: an izar (lower garment) and a rida (upper garment). And he applies perfume if he has perfume. It is called ihram because it prohibits the permissible things before it, like perfume, wearing sewn garments, etc. His saying: 'And he wears two new garments or washed ones' - new is better because it is closer to purity from sins, and for this reason the author (al-Quduri) mentioned it before washed. If he wears only one garment, it suffices him, because the purpose is covering the 'awrah without sewn garments; he mentioned two because the muhrim is prohibited from wearing sewn garments, and he must cover his 'awrah and protect from heat and cold, and that is achieved only by the izar and rida. His saying: 'And he applies perfume if he has perfume.' This indicates that perfume is among the sunan of extras (sunan al-zawa'id), not among the sunan of guidance (sunan al-huda). And the effect of perfume after ihram does not harm. It is reported from Muhammad: he dislikes that one apply perfume whose essence remains after ihram. We said: the initial application of perfume occurred from a permissible source, so its persistence does not harm him, like shaving (before ihram), and because what is prohibited is applying perfume after ihram. And Muhammad says: persistence has the same ruling as initiation, as in wearing a shirt: if he wears it before ihram and does not remove it afterwards.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage explains the recommended acts (sunan) for someone intending to enter ihram for Hajj or Umrah. It begins with the ruling that performing ghusl or wudu before ihram is sunnah, with ghusl being superior. A key point is that this ghusl is for cleanliness (nazafah) and not for ritual purity (taharah), so even a menstruating or postpartum woman is instructed to perform it. Next, the pilgrim is to wear two new or washed garments: an izar (lower wrap) and a rida (upper wrap). New garments are preferred as they are closer to purity from sins. If only one garment is worn, it is still valid as long as it covers the 'awrah and is not sewn. The author mentions two because the muhrim is prohibited from sewn garments and needs to cover the body and protect from elements. Finally, applying perfume before ihram is a sunnah from the category of 'sunan al-zawa'id' (extra recommended acts), not from the core 'sunan al-huda'. The lingering effect of perfume after ihram does not harm the validity of ihram. There is a reported view from Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani that it is makruh to apply perfume that leaves a visible residue after ihram. The Hanafi response (from Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) argues that the initial application was permissible, so the persistence is not problematic, analogizing to shaving the head before ihram where the effect remains. Muhammad draws an analogy to wearing a shirt before ihram and continuing to wear it after, which is prohibited. However, the majority position is that the prohibition is on the act of applying perfume after ihram, not on the state of having the scent, so there is no issue.

Key Terms
الغسل (ghusl): Full body wash; here it is a recommended act before ihram for cleanliness, not ritual purification.
الحائض (al-ha'id): A woman experiencing menstruation.
النفساء (al-nafsa): A woman experiencing postpartum bleeding.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a menstruating woman recommended to perform ghusl before ihram according to the Hanafi school?
A) To purify herself from ritual impurity
B) To follow a sunnah act that does not require ritual purity
C) Only if she can become pure
D) It is not recommended for her

Key Takeaway
Ghusl before ihram is a sunnah act for cleanliness, applicable to all pilgrims including those in a state of ritual impurity.

The Garments of Ihram: Two Pieces or One?
Ruling on ihram garments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the recommended dress for ihram and explain the legal reasoning for preferring two garments over one.

Core Idea
The muhrim should wear two unsewn garments (izar and rida), but one garment suffices if it covers the 'awrah.

This lesson addresses the dress code for ihram. The Hanafi school recommends two white unsewn garments, but the minimum requirement is covering the 'awrah. Understanding the rationale helps in applying the rules in various circumstances.

Guided Path
1
Identify the recommended dress
The text recommends two new or washed garments: an izar (lower wrap) and a rida (upper wrap). New garments are preferred.
2
Understand the purpose
These garments serve to cover the 'awrah and protect from heat and cold, while avoiding sewn garments which are prohibited in ihram.
3
Note the minimum requirement
If only one garment is available, it suffices as long as it covers the 'awrah. The recommendation of two is for better covering and compliance with sunnah.
4
Analyze the preference for new garments
New garments are better because they are cleaner and closer to purity from sins, as mentioned in the commentary.

This passage explains the recommended acts (sunan) for someone intending to enter ihram for Hajj or Umrah. Applying Perfume before Ihram and the Dispute on Lingering Scent
Intra-Hanafi dispute on perfume residue

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on perfume before ihram, explain the intra-Hanafi dispute, and apply the principle of 'al-baqa' lahu hukm al-ibtida'.

Core Idea
Perfume before ihram is sunnah, and its lingering scent does not harm; the majority Hanafi view differs from Imam Muhammad on whether residue matters.

This lesson explores the ruling on applying perfume before entering ihram. While it is a recommended act, there is an important intra-Hanafi disagreement on whether the lingering scent after ihram is problematic. Understanding this debate deepens your grasp of how rulings are derived through analogy and counter-analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sunnah status of perfume
The text classifies perfume as one of the 'sunan al-zawa'id' (extra recommended acts), not from the core sunan al-huda.
2
State the majority Hanafi position
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that applying perfume before ihram is permissible, and its lingering scent after ihram does not affect the validity.
3
Understand Imam Muhammad's position
Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani holds that it is makruh to apply perfume that leaves a visible/oily residue after ihram.
4
Analyze the proofs
Majority argues: initial application was permissible, so persistence is harmless, like shaving before ihram. Muhammad argues: persistence has the ruling of initiation (al-baqa' lahu hukm al-ibtida'), like wearing a sewn shirt before ihram.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: according to majority, no karahah (dislike) for lingering scent; according to Muhammad, it is makruh. The mufta bihi is the majority view.

Wearing Two Garments for Ihram: Sunnah of Izar and Rida
Ruling statement on sunnah garments for iḥrām

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the sunnah of wearing two garments for iḥrām, identify the preferable types (new vs. washed), and explain why a single garment is valid but incomplete.

Core Idea
Wearing two Ihram garments is a sunnah muakkadah — one suffices but both complete the sunnah.

This lesson examines the sunnah of wearing an izār and ridā’ for iḥrām. The passage clarifies that the garments may be new or washed, with new being better. It also addresses the minimal requirement: covering the ‘awrah with a single unsewn garment is sufficient for validity, but the recommended practice (sunnah) is to wear both.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Wearing two garments (izār and ridā’) is a sunnah muakkadah of iḥrām, not a wājib.
The passage explicitly states that one garment suffices.
2
Determine the preferable type
New garments are better than washed because they symbolize purity from sins.
The author gives precedence to new garments.
3
Understand the minimal validity
One garment suffices for valid iḥrām because the essential requirement is covering the ‘awrah with non-sewn cloth.
The passage says 'if he wears a single garment, it suffices him.'
4
Recognize the rationale for two garments
Two garments fulfill covering, protection from weather, and avoidance of sewn clothing.
The muḥrim is prohibited from sewn garments, so unsewn wraps are needed.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Some schools require two garments as a condition; Hanafis consider it sunnah.
This difference does not affect the validity of iḥrām.

And he wears two new garments, or two washed ones: an izār and a ridā’. He said: (And he wears two new garments, or two washed ones). The new is better because it is closer to purity from sins, and for this reason the Shaykh gave it precedence over washed garments. If he wears a single garment, it suffices him, because the purpose is covering the nakedness with non-sewn garments. He only mentioned two garments because the muḥrim is prohibited from wearing sewn garments, and he must cover his nakedness and ward off heat and cold, and that is achieved only by the izār and ridā’.

Commentary
This passage states the sunnah of wearing two unsewn garments for iḥrām: an izār (lower wrap) and a ridā’ (upper wrap). The garments should be new or washed; new is preferable as it symbolizes purity from sins. If only one garment is worn, the iḥrām is still valid because the essential requirement is covering the ‘awrah without sewn clothing. The mention of two garments is because they fulfill the needs of covering and protection from weather, while also avoiding the prohibited sewn garments. This ruling is from the sunan of iḥrām, not a fard.

Key Terms

رداء (ridā’): An upper garment draped over the shoulders and covering the upper body
غسيل (ghasīl): A garment that has been washed, as opposed to new

Applying Perfume Before Ihram – The Majority and Muhammad's View
Ruling on perfume before ihram with intra-Hanafi dispute on remaining trace

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two Hanafi positions on applying perfume before ihram and state the relied-upon ruling with its rational proof.

Core Idea
Perfume before ihram is sunnah; its lingering trace does not harm – the majority's qiyas vs Muhammad's qiyas.

This passage from al-Qudūrī's Mukhtasar explains the ruling on applying perfume before entering ihram. It highlights an intra-Hanafi disagreement: the majority (Abū Ḥanīfa and Abū Yūsuf) permit it, while Imam Muḥammad al-Shaybānī considers it makrūh if the scent remains. The dispute turns on whether the continuance of a permissible act is treated like a new initiation. We will dissect both arguments and see which is deemed stronger in the school.

Guided Path
1
State the majority Hanafi position
Identify the ruling of the majority (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) on applying perfume before ihram and the effect of its trace remaining.
The majority holds it is sunnah and the trace does not harm.
2
Identify the intra-Hanafi variant
Point out Imam Muhammad's opposing view: he considers it makruh to apply perfume whose essence remains after ihram.
This is a valid difference within the school.
3
Analyze the majority's proof
Examine the two arguments given: (1) the initial act was permissible, so its continuation does not harm, analogous to shaving the head; (2) the prohibition is only on new application after ihram.
The analogy to shaving shows that a permissible act's result (hair growth) does not become prohibited.
4
Analyze Muhammad's proof
Explain Muhammad's analogical argument: the continuance has the same ruling as initiation, just like wearing a sewn garment before ihram and not removing it afterwards.
For sewn garments, wearing before ihram and continuing is prohibited because the garment itself is prohibited in ihram.
5
Determine the thamarah and tarjih
State the practical difference: those who follow Muhammad will avoid perfumes with lasting scent; the majority see no harm. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is the majority view.
Tarjih is based on the difference between the nature of perfume and sewn garments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومَسَ طِيْباً إِنْ كان له طِيْبٌ .
وسمي الإحرام؛ لأنه يُحرِّم المباحات قبله من الطيب، ولبس المخيط، وغير ذلك.
قوله : (ومَسَّ طِيْباً إن كان له طيب).
هذا يدل على أن الطّيب من سنن الزوائد، وليس من سنن الهدى.
ولا يضرُّ أثر الطيب بعد الإحرام.
وعن محمد: يكره أن يتطيب بما تبقى عينه بعد الإحرام. قلنا : ابتداء الطيب حصل من وجه مباح، فالبقاء عليه لا يضره، كالحلق، ولأن الممنوع منه : التطيب بعد الإحرام.
ومحمد يقول: للبقاء حكم الابتداء، كما في لبس القميص إذا لبسه قبل الإحرام، ولم يخلعه بعده.
And he applies perfume if he has perfume. And it is called iḥrām because it prohibits permissible things before it, like perfume, wearing sewn garments, and so on. He said: (And he applies perfume if he has perfume). This indicates that perfume is from the additional sunan (sunan al-zawā’id), not from the sunan of guidance (sunan al-hudā). And the trace of perfume after iḥrām does not harm him. And it is reported from Muḥammad: it is disliked (makrūh) to apply perfume whose essence remains after iḥrām. We said: the initial application of perfume occurred from a permissible source, so its continuance does not harm him, like shaving (the head), and because what is prohibited is applying perfume after iḥrām. And Muḥammad says: the continuance has the ruling of initiation, like wearing a shirt if he wore it before iḥrām and did not remove it afterward.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on applying perfume before entering iḥrām. The author states that it is sunnah to apply perfume if one has it, and this is classified as a sunnah from the 'sunan al-zawā’id' (additional recommended acts), not from the 'sunan al-hudā' (essential sunan related to guidance). The key ruling is that if one applies perfume before iḥrām, the lingering trace of that perfume after iḥrām does not invalidate it. However, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Muḥammad al-Shaybānī holds that it is makrūh to apply perfume whose scent remains after iḥrām, because he considers the continuation of the action to have the same ruling as its initiation. The majority Hanafi position (Abū Ḥanīfa and Abū Yūsuf) argues that since the initial act was permissible (before iḥrām), its continuation is not harmful, analogous to shaving the head before iḥrām and the hair remaining after. The prohibited act is applying perfume after iḥrām, not the trace of a previous application. This is a matter of qiyās: the continuance is not like the initiation. The relied-upon position (muftā bihī) is the majority view that it is not makrūh.

Key Terms
سنن الزوائد (sunan al-zawā’id): Additional recommended acts that are not essential; performing them is meritorious but omitting them is not sinful.
طيب (ṭīb): Perfume or any pleasant scent applied to the body.
بقاء العين (baqā' al-'ayn): The remaining physical essence or trace of the perfume after it is applied.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what is the ruling on applying perfume before iḥrām if its trace remains after entering iḥrām?
A) It is makrūh.
B) It is permissible and not disliked.
C) It is haram.
D) It invalidates the iḥrām.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's relied-upon opinion is that applying perfume before ihram is sunnah and its lingering trace after ihram does not harm; Imam Muhammad's makruh ruling is a minority view within the school.

The Sunnahs Before Ihram: Two Rak'ahs and Dua
Explains the two-rak'ah prayer and du'a before ihram.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the recommended actions before ihram and apply the Hanafi ruling that they are sunnah, not wajib.

Core Idea
The two rak'ahs before ihram are sunnah; the supplication seeks ease for the journey.

Before embarking on the journey of Hajj, the Muslim is encouraged to perform two rak'ahs and then to supplicate for ease and acceptance. This lesson unpacks the details of this sunnah as taught by the Hanafi school, explaining both the recitation and the wisdom behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the recommended acts
The passage mentions two actions: praying two rak'ahs and making a specific supplication after the prayer. Note that these are done before entering the state of ihram.
The prayer is performed before tying the ihram.
2
Determine the legal status
In the Hanafi school, these acts are classified as sunnah, not wajib. Omitting them does not invalidate the ihram.
The text does not use terms like 'fard' or 'wajib'; it simply describes what the pilgrim does.
3
Analyze the recitation
The first rak'ah: al-Fātiḥah + Sūrat al-Kāfirūn. The second rak'ah: al-Fātiḥah + Sūrat al-Ikhlāṣ. The commentator links this to seeking help through patience and prayer (Qur'an 2:45).
This recitation is a sunnah within the sunnah prayer.
4
Understand the supplication
After the prayer, the pilgrim says: 'O Allah, I intend Hajj, so make it easy for me and accept it from me.' This is said immediately after the prayer ('uqayb al-ṣalāh).
The supplication is not part of the prayer itself; it is a separate recommended act.
5
Recognize the wisdom
Hajj involves travel, different times, and places, and is not free from difficulty. Hence the specific supplication for ease. Prayer and fasting at home do not require such a supplication because they are done in familiar settings.
This illustrates the principle that harder acts warrant more supplication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصلى ركعتين، وقال عَقِيبَ الصلاة : اللهم إني أريــد الـحـج، فيسره لي، وتقبله مني .
قوله: (وصلى ركعتين)، يقرأ في الأولى الفاتحة، و: (قُلْ يَتَأَيُّهَا الكَفِرُونَ )، وفي الثانية الفاتحة، و: قُلْ هُوَ اللهُ أَحَدٌ ). والمعنى بذلك: الإشارة إلى قوله تعالى: ﴿وَاسْتَعِينُوا بِالصَّبْرِ وَالصَّلَوةِ . البقرة/ ٤٥ ، ويسأل الله الإعانة والتوفيق في جميع أموره.
قوله: (وقال عقيب الصلاة: اللهم إني أريد الحج فيسره لي، وتقبله مني).
وإنما لم يُذكر مثل هذا الدعاء في الصلاة والصوم؛ لأن الحج يُؤدَّى في أزمنة متفرقة، وأماكن متباينة، فلا يعرى عن المشقة، فيسأل الله تعالى التيسير.
And he prays two rak'ahs, and says after the prayer: 'O Allah, I intend Hajj, so make it easy for me and accept it from me.' He said: (And he prays two rak'ahs). He recites in the first rak'ah al-Fātiḥah and 'Say, O disbelievers,' and in the second rak'ah al-Fātiḥah and 'Say, He is Allah, the One.' The meaning of that is to point to the saying of Allah: 'And seek help through patience and prayer' (Qur’an 2:45), and he asks Allah for assistance and success in all his affairs. He said: (And says after the prayer: 'O Allah, I intend Hajj, so make it easy for me and accept it from me.') And such a supplication was not mentioned for prayer and fasting because Hajj is performed at different times and places and is not free from difficulty, so he asks Allah for ease.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage outlines the sunnah of praying two rak'ahs before entering the state of iḥrām. The recitation in the prayer is specified: in the first rak'ah, Sūrat al-Fātiḥah followed by Sūrat al-Kāfirūn; in the second rak'ah, Sūrat al-Fātiḥah followed by Sūrat al-Ikhlāṣ. After completing the prayer, the pilgrim should recite the supplication: 'O Allah, I intend Hajj, so make it easy for me and accept it from me.' The reason for this specific supplication for Hajj is that Hajj involves travel and hardships, unlike daily prayers and fasting which are easier to perform. This prayer and supplication are considered mustaḥabb (recommended) according to the Hanafi school. The commentator explains that the recitation of these surahs alludes to seeking Allah's help through patience and prayer, as mentioned in Qur’an 2:45.

Key Terms
ركعتين (rak‘atayn): Two units of prayer, specifically the sunnah prayer before iḥrām.
عقيب الصلاة (‘uqayb al-ṣalāh): Immediately after the prayer, without a long interval.
يسره (yassirhu): Make it easy; a plea for facilitation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Immediately after the taslim of the prayer
C) After entering the state of ihram
D) At the time of seeing the Kaaba

Key Takeaway
The two rak'ahs and supplication before ihram are sunnah acts that emphasize seeking divine aid for the challenging journey of Hajj.

Timing of the Talbiyah and the Necessity of Intention in Hajj Ifrād
Timing of talbiyah and the necessity of niyyah for ifrād Hajj

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the sunnah timing of the talbiyah and explain why intention is required for a pilgrim performing ifrād Hajj.

Core Idea
The talbiyah must be accompanied by intention to specify the type of pilgrimage, and its earliest sunnah time is right after the prayer.

This lesson clarifies the recommended timing for beginning the talbiyah and the essential role of intention (niyyah) for a pilgrim entering iḥrām exclusively for Hajj (ifrād). The source text, from Al-Jawhara al-Nayyira, distinguishes between the optimal and permissible timing and grounds the necessity of intention in the general principle 'actions are by intentions.'

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage discusses the talbiyah (recitation of Labbayk) and its timing. The act is the recital of the talbiyah, which is a sunnah act of iḥrām.
2
Distinguish between recommended and permissible timing
The optimal time is immediately after the two rak'ah prayer at the mīqāt. Delaying until after mounting the ride is still permissible but less preferred (afḍal).
This shows the Hanafi principle of al-afḍaliyyah (preference) in sunnah acts.
3
Recognize the requirement of intention for ifrād Hajj
For a pilgrim performing Hajj alone (mufrid), an explicit intention (niyyah) for Hajj must accompany the talbiyah. Without it, the iḥrām is not valid.
The intention differentiates ifrād from tamattu‘ or qirān.
4
Connect to the underlying principle
The text cites the hadith 'innamā al-a'mālu bil-niyyāt' (actions are judged by intentions) to justify the necessity of niyyah. This is a foundational principle in Hanafi fiqh.
Intention is a condition (shart) for the validity of iḥrām.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يلبي عَقِيب صلاته، فإن كان مُفرِداً بالحج : نوى بتلبيته الحج.
قوله: (ثم يلبي عقيب صلاته).
فإن لبى بعد ما استوت به راحلته: جاز، ولكن الأول أفضل.
قوله: (فإن كان مفرداً بالحج: نوى بتلبيته الحج)؛ لأنها عبادة، والأعمال بالنيات.
Then he recites the talbiyah immediately after his prayer. And if he is performing Hajj alone (ifrād), he intends Hajj with his talbiyah. He said: (Then he recites the talbiyah immediately after his prayer). If he recites the talbiyah after his mount has stood up, it is permissible, but the first is better. He said: (If he is performing Hajj alone, he intends Hajj with his talbiyah) because it is an act of worship, and actions are judged by intentions.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage discusses the timing of the talbiyah (the proclamation of Labbayk) and the accompanying intention. The recommended time to begin the talbiyah is immediately after the two-rak'ah prayer described earlier. However, if one delays until after mounting his ride, it is still permissible, but the earlier timing is superior. For someone performing Hajj in the ifrād manner (Hajj alone, without 'Umrah), he must make the intention for Hajj at the time of talbiyah. This is because the talbiyah itself is an act of worship, and according to the hadith 'Actions are judged by intentions,' the intention is required to distinguish which type of pilgrimage is intended. The Hanafi school emphasizes the necessity of intention (niyyah) for the validity of iḥrām.

Key Terms
تلبية (talbiyah): The proclamation 'Labbayk Allāhumma labbayk...' that announces the pilgrim's entry into iḥrām.
مفرداً بالحج (mufridan bi al-ḥajj): One who performs Hajj alone (ifrād) without combining it with 'Umrah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) It is permissible but less preferred.

Key Takeaway
The optimal time for talbiyah is immediately after the prayer, and intention is required to specify the type of pilgrimage.

Recommended Actions Before Ihram: Prayer and Supplication
Describes the recommended prayer, du'a, and talbiyah before ihram.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the recommended actions (sunan) before entering ihram and explain the timing and intention for the talbiyah.

Core Idea
The two-rak'ah prayer and du'a are sunnah; the talbiyah initiates ihram and requires intention for ifrad.

Before embarking on the sacred journey of Hajj, the Hanafi school prescribes a set of recommended acts to spiritually prepare and seek Allah's assistance. This lesson walks through the two-rak'ah prayer, the supplication, and the timing of the talbiyah, including the specific intention for one performing Hajj alone (ifrad).

Guided Path
1
Identify the recommended prayer
The pilgrim prays two rak'ahs after making the intention for Hajj, reciting Surah Al-Kafirun and Al-Ikhlas.
The recitation follows the Prophet's practice.
2
Note the supplication
After prayer, a specific du'a is recited: 'Allahumma inni uridu al-hajja...' asking for ease and acceptance.
This du'a is mentioned because Hajj involves hardship.
3

The talbiyah should ideally follow the prayer immediately, but it is permissible to delay until the mount is ready.
Immediate recitation is better (afdal).
4
Distinguish the intention for ifrad
For one performing Hajj alone (mufrid), the intention of Hajj is made with the talbiyah.
Because actions are by intentions.

The Talbiyah: Wording and Obligation
Defines the talbiyah and states its obligatory status and addition rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the exact wording of the talbiyah, explain its wajib status in the Hanafi school, and apply the ruling on adding extra phrases.

Core Idea
The Hanafi talbiyah is wajib; its exact wording must be preserved, though additions after completion are allowed.

The talbiyah is the pilgrim's declaration of presence before Allah. In the Hanafi school, it holds a unique status: it is wajib, not merely sunnah, and its wording is fixed by Prophetic practice. This lesson unpacks the ruling, the evidence, and the boundaries of personal additions, drawing from the classical commentary of Al-Jawhara Al-Nayra.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the talbiyah is a specific invocation recited upon entering ihram. According to the Hanafi school, it is wajib (obligatory).
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the constant practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him) as transmitted through agreed-upon narrations. The exact wording is given in the source.
The dalil is a hadith ahad but considered strong by the Hanafis.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Hanafi school derives wujub from the Prophet's consistent practice and the fact that the wording is transmitted without variation, indicating a required formula.
Compare with Shafi'i view that talbiyah is sunnah.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The Shafi'i school holds talbiyah to be sunnah, not wajib. The Hanafi response is that the act of driving a sacrificial animal can substitute for talbiyah, showing its obligatory nature.
This substitution (sawq al-hady) is unique Hanafi evidence.
5
Evaluate the addition ruling
The text allows adding phrases after completing the talbiyah, but not during it. Additions like those of Ibn Umar are permissible as long as the original wording is intact.
This balances adherence to sunnah with personal devotion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتلبية أن يقول : لَبَّيْكَ اللهمَّ لَبَّيْكَ، لَبَّيْكَ لا شريك لك لبيك، إن الحمد والنعمة لك والملك، لا شريك لك .
ولا ينبغي أن يُخل بشيء من هذه الكلمات .
فإذا زاد فيها : جاز .
قوله: (والتلبية أن يقول : لبيك اللهم لبيك، لبيك لا شريك لك لبيك إن الحمد والنعمة لك والملك، لا شريك لك).
وهذه تلبية رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم
(1)
وهي واجبة عندنا، أو ما قام مقامها من سوق الهدي.
ولو كان مكان التلبية تسبيح، أو تهليل، أو ما أشبه ذلك من ذكر الله ، ونوى به الإحرام: صار مُحرِماً.
* قوله: (ولا ينبغي أن يُخِلَّ بشيءٍ من هذه الكلمات)؛ لأنها تلبيه رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم، باتفاق الرواة، فلا ينقص منها. * قوله: (فإن زاد فيها : (جاز)، يعني بعد الإتيان بها، أما في خلالها : فلا. وكان ابن عمر رضي الله عنهما يزيد في تلبيته: لبيك وسعديك، والخير كله في يديك، والرَّغباء إليك، لبيك لبيك (٢).
وزاد بعضهم: لبيك حقاً حقاً، لبيك تعبداً ورقاً.
And the talbiyah is to say: 'Labbayk Allahumma labbayk, labbayka la sharika laka labbayk, inna al-hamda wa al-ni'mata laka wa al-mulk, la sharika lak.' And one should not omit anything from these words. And if he adds to them, it is permissible. Commentary on his saying: (And the talbiyah is to say...). And this is the talbiyah of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) (1). And it is obligatory in our school, or what takes its place such as driving the sacrificial animal. And if instead of talbiyah he says tasbih, tahlil, or similar remembrance of Allah and intends ihram, he becomes a muhrim. Commentary on his saying: (And one should not omit anything from these words) because it is the talbiyah of the Messenger of Allah, agreed upon by the narrators, so nothing should be omitted from it. Commentary on his saying: (And if he adds to them, it is permissible), meaning after completing it, but during it, no. And Ibn Umar (may Allah be pleased with them) used to add in his talbiyah: 'Labbayka wa sa'dayka, wa al-khayru kulluhu fi yadayka, wa al-raghba'u ilayka, labbayka labbayk' (2). And some added: 'Labbayka haqqan haqqan, labbayka ta'abbudan wa riqqan.'
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage from Al-Jawhara Al-Nayra discusses the talbiyah, the proclamation of intent by the pilgrim. The author specifies the exact wording as transmitted from the Prophet (peace be upon him). In the Hanafi school, the talbiyah is wajib (obligatory) for the validity of ihram, or at least its substitute such as driving a sacrificial animal (for those performing qiran or tamattu). If one says other forms of dhikr with the intention of ihram, it still counts as ihram. The author notes that one should not omit any of the words of the talbiyah because it is agreed upon by narrators. However, adding extra phrases after completing the talbiyah is permissible, as reported from Ibn Umar who used to add 'Labbayka wa sa'dayka...' Some even added 'Labbayka haqqan haqqan...' but adding during the talbiyah itself (in the middle) is not allowed. This shows the importance of following the Prophet's exact wording while allowing supplemental invocations.

Key Terms
التلبية (al-talbiyah): The specific invocation recited by the pilgrim upon entering ihram, starting with 'Labbayk Allahumma labbayk'
لَبَّيْكَ (labbayk): A phrase meaning 'I am present at Your service', repeated in the talbiyah
سوق الهدي (sawq al-hady): Driving the sacrificial animal to the Haram as a substitute for talbiyah

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Sunnah mu'akkadah
B) Wajib
C) Fard
D) Mubah

Key Takeaway
The talbiyah is wajib in the Hanafi school, must be recited exactly as transmitted, but personal additions are allowed after completion.

The Talbiyah: Wording, Ruling, and Additions
Explains the obligatory wording of Talbiyah and permissibility of additions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the obligatory wording of the Talbiyah, identify its Hanafi ruling as wajib, and explain when additions are permissible.

Core Idea
The Talbiyah is wajib: recite the Prophet's exact words, but you may add supplications after completing.

The Talbiyah is the declaration of intention that initiates the state of ihram. In the Hanafi school, it is a wajib — an obligation that requires compensation if omitted but does not invalidate the hajj. This passage from the sharh of al-Quduri details the exact wording transmitted from the Prophet ﷺ and addresses common questions about whether one may add extra phrases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is reciting the Talbiyah upon entering ihram. According to the Hanafi school, it is wajib, meaning its omission requires a dam (sacrifice) but does not invalidate the hajj.
The text states 'وهي واجبة عندنا' (it is obligatory with us).
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the Prophet's own practice. The text says 'وهذه تلبية رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم' (this is the Talbiyah of the Messenger of Allah). The narrators unanimously transmitted this wording.
The hadith is found in Sahih al-Bukhari and Muslim.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's continuous practice and his command 'خذوا عني مناسككم' (learn your rituals from me) indicate wujub in Hanafi usul. The verb 'يقول' (he says) in the description implies a requirement.
The Hanafi principle: af'al al-nabi 'ala al-wujub at the level of dalalah zanni.
4
Note the flexibility in substitution
The text allows that if one recites tasbih, tahlil, or similar dhikr with the intention of ihram, it suffices for entering ihram even if not the exact talbiyah wording.
This shows that the wajib is reciting the talbiyah or its equivalent in meaning if intention is present.
5
Understand the addition rule
Adding words after completing the talbiyah is permissible, as shown by Ibn Umar's practice. Adding during the talbiyah (before finishing) is not recommended.
The text says 'فإن زاد فيها : (جاز)، يعني بعد الإتيان بها، أما في خلالها : فلا'.

And Ibn 'Umar, may Allah be pleased with them both, used to add in his talbiyah: 'Labbaika wa sa'daik, wal-khayru kulluhu fi yadaik, war-raghba'u ilaik, labbaika labbaik.' (2). Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Quduri discusses the Talbiyah, its wording, ruling, and permissible additions. The author states that the prescribed wording of the Talbiyah is that reported from the Prophet ﷺ, and it is considered obligatory (wajib) in the Hanafi school. However, if one substitutes other forms of dhikr (like tasbih or tahlil) with the intention of ihram, that is sufficient. The text warns against omitting any words of the Talbiyah because the narrators unanimously transmitted it from the Prophet. Adding words after completing the Talbiyah is permissible, as shown by the practice of Ibn 'Umar who added supplications, and others who added phrases like 'haqqan haqqan'. Adding during the Talbiyah itself is not recommended.

Key Terms
التلبية (al-talbiyah): The specific formula recited by the muhrim to proclaim intention for Hajj/Umrah: 'Labbaik Allahumma labbaik...'
واجبة (wajibah): A duty whose omission requires compensation (dam) but does not invalidate the hajj.
الإحرام (al-ihram): The state of ritual consecration for Hajj/Umrah, entered by intention and talbiyah.

Prohibitions of Ihram: Rafath, Fusuq, Jidal, and Hunting
Lists prohibitions of ihram with Quranic proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four categories of prohibitions during ihram and identify the Quranic evidence for each.

Core Idea
Four acts are forbidden in ihram: rafath, fusuq, jidal, and hunting; each is directly prohibited by the Quran.

Upon entering ihram, the pilgrim must observe a set of prohibitions. This passage from Al-Jawhara al-Nayyira lists four key categories: rafath (sexual/obscene conduct), fusuq (all sins), jidal (argumentation), and hunting (sayd). Each has a clear textual basis. Understanding these prohibitions helps maintain the sanctity of ihram.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four prohibited acts
Name rafath, fusuq, jidal, and sayd from the text. These are the specific actions forbidden after entering ihram.
Rafath is defined first as intercourse, then as foul speech.
2
Locate the Quranic proof for each
For rafath, the text cites Quran 2:187 (mention of rafath in context of fasting). For hunting, Quran 5:95 is quoted. Fusuq and jidal are derived from general commands to avoid sin and argument, though not explicitly quoted.
The explicit proof for hunting is the verse 'Wa la taqtulu al-sayda wa antum hurum'.
3
Define each term per the Hanafi school
Rafath includes both sexual intercourse and obscene speech; fusuq encompasses all sins, more severe in ihram; jidal is argumentation leading to anger; sayd is any wild animal by original nature, edible or not.
The Hanafi definition of sayd includes all untamed animals, not just edible ones.
4
Note the specific ruling
All four are haram. For hunting, violation requires compensation (dam). The others incur sin but do not invalidate ihram unless they involve intercourse (rafath).
The text does not detail penalties, but later fiqh works specify dam for hunting.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
A pilgrim must avoid these acts. For example, arguing angrily with a companion is jidal, which is haram, and requires repentance.
The prohibitions apply from the moment of ihram until exiting it.

Commentary
This passage describes the prohibitions of ihram after entering the state. The author first emphasizes that ihram requires both talbiyah (or similar dhikr) and intention; intention alone is insufficient. Finally, hunting is prohibited by explicit Quranic verse (5:95). The definition of game (sayd) includes all wild animals, whether edible or not, based on their original nature being untamed and inaccessible.

الجدال (al-jidal): Argumentation that leads to anger; prohibited during ihram.
الصيد (al-sayd): Any wild animal that is untamed by original nature, whether edible or not.

Prohibition of Killing Game in Ihram
Explaining the prohibition of killing game and defining sayd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on killing game during ihram, cite the Quranic proof, and define sayd according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Killing wild game is haram for the muhrim; the prohibition covers all wild animals, edible or not.

In this lesson, we examine a foundational ruling from the Hajj chapter: the prohibition of hunting or killing wild game while in the state of ihram. The commentator not only cites the direct Quranic verse but also provides a precise legal definition of 'sayd' (game), which determines the scope of the prohibition. Understanding this definition is crucial to know which animals are included and which are not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is killing game (sayd) while in ihram. The ruling is haram (prohibited).
This is a clear prohibition based on the Quranic verse.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the Quranic ayah 5:95: 'Do not kill game while you are in a state of ihram.'
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The imperative 'la taqtulu' indicates a clear prohibition (nahy), which in Hanafi usul signifies haram.
4
Understand the definition of sayd
The text defines sayd as any wild, untamed animal by original creation, whether edible or not. Domesticated animals are excluded.
5
Apply the definition
Distinguish between a wild deer (sayd) and a domestic chicken (not sayd). The prohibition applies only to the former.
This definition is essential for determining what is prohibited.

Source Text

قوله: (ولا يقتل صيداً) لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَا تَقْتُلُوا الصَّيْدَ وَأَنتُمْ حُرُمٌ﴾ المائدة/٩٥، أي : وأنتم محرمون، وحرم: جمع: حرام.

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof from the Quran prohibiting the killing of game during ihram. The commentator quotes verse 5:95, explaining that 'hurum' means those in ihram. He then provides a legal definition of 'sayd' (game): any animal that is inherently wild and unattainable (i.e., not domesticated), regardless of whether it is lawful to eat. This definition is important because domesticated animals are not considered game for the purpose of this prohibition. The ruling is based on the explicit Quranic command and is a clear prohibition.

Key Terms
صيد (sayd): Wild, untamed animals that are unattainable by nature, whether edible or not.
حرُم (hurum): Plural of haram, meaning those in the state of ihram.

Prohibition of Pointing and Indicating Game While in Ihram
Definition and prohibition of pointing and indicating game

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between pointing (isharah) and indicating (dalalah) and state the prohibition of both actions while in ihram.

Core Idea
Pointing or indicating game to another is prohibited even if no kill follows.

The muhrim is prohibited from assisting in the killing of game in any way, including pointing with the hand or indicating verbally. This lesson defines the two actions and establishes the general prohibition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two prohibited actions
Recognize that the text prohibits both isharah (pointing with the hand) and dalalah (verbal indication).
Isharah is for game that is present; dalalah is for game that is absent.
2
Distinguish between isharah and dalalah
Isharah is done with the hand when the game is visible; dalalah is done with the tongue when the game is hidden.
The commentator explicitly ties isharah to presence and dalalah to absence.
3
Recognize the independence of the prohibition
The prohibition stands regardless of whether a kill actually occurs; it is a sin in itself.
This counters the misconception that prohibition depends on the outcome.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ولا يُشير إليه) : أي بيده.
قوله: (ولا) يَدلُّ عليه أي بلسانه لا يقول: في موضع فلان صيد.
فالإشارة تختص بالحضرة، والدلالة: بالغيبة.
ولو قال محرم لحلال : خلف هذا الحائط صيد، فإذا هي صيود كثيرة، فأخذها، وقتلها: فعلى الدال في ذلك كله الجزاء.
بخلاف ما إذا رأى من الصيد واحداً، فدله عليه، فإذا عنده صيود غيره، فقتلها المدلول عليه : فليس على الدال إلا جزاء الصيد الذي دل عليه. - ثم الدلالة إنما تعمل إذا اتصل بها القبض، وأن لا يكون المدلول عالماً بمكان الصيد، وأن يُصدقه في دلالته، ويتبعه في أثره أما إذا كذَّبه في الدلالة، ولم يتبع أثره، حتى دله آخر، فصدقه، واتبع أثره، فقتله : فلا جزاء على الدال الأول.
فدله
ولو رأى المحرم صيداً في موضع لا يقدر عليه إلا أن يرميه بشيء، محرم آخر على قوس ونشاب، أو دفع إليه ذلك، فرماه، فقتله:
فعلى كل واحد منهما الجزاء.
ولو استعار محرم من محرم سكيناً ليذبح بها صيداً فذبح الصيد : فلا جزاء على صاحب السكين.
وقيل : عليه الجزاء.
معه ،
فأعاره ،
His saying: (And he should not point to it): meaning with his hand. His saying: (And he should not indicate it): meaning with his tongue, he should not say 'In such a place is game'. So pointing is specific to presence, and indication to absence. And if a muhrim says to a non-muhrim: 'Behind this wall is game', and there are many game animals, and he takes and kills them: then the one who indicated is liable for all of it. Unlike if he saw one game animal and indicated it to him, and then there are other game animals with it, and the one indicated kills them: then the one who indicated is only liable for the game he indicated. Then indication only becomes effective if it is followed by seizure, and that the one indicated was not already aware of the game's location, and that he believes his indication and follows him. But if he denies his indication and does not follow him, until another indicates to him, and he believes and follows and kills: then there is no liability on the first indicator. And if a muhrim sees game in a place that he cannot reach except that another muhrim throws something at it with a bow and arrow, or he gives that to him and he shoots and kills it: then each one of them is liable. And if a muhrim borrows a knife from another muhrim to slaughter game with it and slaughters the game: then there is no liability on the owner of the knife. And it is said: he is liable.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement detailing the prohibitions of pointing (isharah) and indicating (dalalah) to game while in ihram, and the associated liability (jaza') for the one who indicates. The commentator distinguishes between pointing (for game present) and indicating (for game absent). Several scenarios are presented: (1) If a muhrim tells a non-muhrim that there is game behind a wall, and multiple game are found and killed, the indicator is liable for all. (2) If the indicator indicates a single animal, but the hunter finds multiple, he is only liable for the one indicated. (3) Conditions for liability: the indication must be followed by seizure, the hunter must not already know the location, and the hunter must believe and follow the indication. (4) If two muhrims collaborate in killing game (one points or indicates, the other shoots), both are liable. (5) If a muhrim lends a knife to another to slaughter game, there is a difference of opinion: the relied-upon view is no liability on the lender, but some say he is liable. The commentary follows the matn's order, expanding each point with hypothetical cases.

Key Terms
الإشارة (al-isharah): Pointing to game that is present with the hand.
الدلالة (al-dalalah): Verbally indicating the location of game that is absent.
الجزاء (al-jaza'): The penalty (fidyah or compensation) for killing game while in ihram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is a prohibited action for a muhrim?
A) Pointing with the hand towards game
B) Looking at game without intention
C) Walking past game
D) Thinking about hunting

Key Takeaway
A muhrim must avoid any action that directs another to game, whether by gesture or speech.

Liability for Indicating Game: Conditions and Cases
Detailed scenarios of liability for indicating game

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the conditions for liability (jaza') on the one who indicates game and determine liability in multi-defendant scenarios.

Core Idea
Liability for indicating game requires the hunter to follow the indication and seize the game; multiple indicators may share liability.

When a muhrim indicates game to another, liability for the penalty (jaza') depends on several conditions. This lesson examines the detailed scenarios presented by the commentator, including multiple game, multiple indicators, and joint action.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditions for liability
The text lists three conditions: (1) the indication is followed by seizure (qabd), (2) the hunter did not already know the game's location, (3) the hunter believes the indicator and follows him.
If any condition is missing, the indicator is not liable.
2
Apply to the scenario of multiple game
If a general indication leads to multiple game, the indicator is liable for all; if he indicates a specific animal, he is liable only for that one.
The key is specificity of the indication.
3
Apply to the scenario of multiple indicators
If two muhrims cooperate (one points/shoots, the other provides means), both are liable because both caused the kill.
Direct causation and cooperation trigger shared liability.
4
Analyze the knife-lending case
If a muhrim lends a knife to another to kill game, the lender is not liable (relied-upon view); the borrower's own act is the direct cause.
A minority view holds the lender liable; the majority view is preferred.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ولا يُشير إليه) : أي بيده.
قوله: (ولا) يَدلُّ عليه أي بلسانه لا يقول: في موضع فلان صيد.
فالإشارة تختص بالحضرة، والدلالة: بالغيبة.
ولو قال محرم لحلال : خلف هذا الحائط صيد، فإذا هي صيود كثيرة، فأخذها، وقتلها: فعلى الدال في ذلك كله الجزاء.
بخلاف ما إذا رأى من الصيد واحداً، فدله عليه، فإذا عنده صيود غيره، فقتلها المدلول عليه : فليس على الدال إلا جزاء الصيد الذي دل عليه. - ثم الدلالة إنما تعمل إذا اتصل بها القبض، وأن لا يكون المدلول عالماً بمكان الصيد، وأن يُصدقه في دلالته، ويتبعه في أثره أما إذا كذَّبه في الدلالة، ولم يتبع أثره، حتى دله آخر، فصدقه، واتبع أثره، فقتله : فلا جزاء على الدال الأول.
فدله
ولو رأى المحرم صيداً في موضع لا يقدر عليه إلا أن يرميه بشيء، محرم آخر على قوس ونشاب، أو دفع إليه ذلك، فرماه، فقتله:
فعلى كل واحد منهما الجزاء.
ولو استعار محرم من محرم سكيناً ليذبح بها صيداً فذبح الصيد : فلا جزاء على صاحب السكين.
وقيل : عليه الجزاء.
معه ،
فأعاره ،
His saying: (And he should not point to it): meaning with his hand. His saying: (And he should not indicate it): meaning with his tongue, he should not say 'In such a place is game'. So pointing is specific to presence, and indication to absence. And if a muhrim says to a non-muhrim: 'Behind this wall is game', and there are many game animals, and he takes and kills them: then the one who indicated is liable for all of it. Unlike if he saw one game animal and indicated it to him, and then there are other game animals with it, and the one indicated kills them: then the one who indicated is only liable for the game he indicated. Then indication only becomes effective if it is followed by seizure, and that the one indicated was not already aware of the game's location, and that he believes his indication and follows him. But if he denies his indication and does not follow him, until another indicates to him, and he believes and follows and kills: then there is no liability on the first indicator. And if a muhrim sees game in a place that he cannot reach except that another muhrim throws something at it with a bow and arrow, or he gives that to him and he shoots and kills it: then each one of them is liable. And if a muhrim borrows a knife from another muhrim to slaughter game with it and slaughters the game: then there is no liability on the owner of the knife. And it is said: he is liable.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement detailing the prohibitions of pointing (isharah) and indicating (dalalah) to game while in ihram, and the associated liability (jaza') for the one who indicates. The commentator distinguishes between pointing (for game present) and indicating (for game absent). Several scenarios are presented: (1) If a muhrim tells a non-muhrim that there is game behind a wall, and multiple game are found and killed, the indicator is liable for all. (2) If the indicator indicates a single animal, but the hunter finds multiple, he is only liable for the one indicated. (3) Conditions for liability: the indication must be followed by seizure, the hunter must not already know the location, and the hunter must believe and follow the indication. (4) If two muhrims collaborate in killing game (one points or indicates, the other shoots), both are liable. (5) If a muhrim lends a knife to another to slaughter game, there is a difference of opinion: the relied-upon view is no liability on the lender, but some say he is liable. The commentary follows the matn's order, expanding each point with hypothetical cases.

Key Terms
الإشارة (al-isharah): Pointing to game that is present with the hand.
الدلالة (al-dalalah): Verbally indicating the location of game that is absent.
الجزاء (al-jaza'): The penalty (fidyah or compensation) for killing game while in ihram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a muhrim says 'There is a deer behind that tree' to a hunter who already knows the deer is there, what is the ruling on the muhrim?
A) He is liable for the deer if killed
B) He is not liable because the hunter already knew
C) He is liable because he indicated
D) He is liable only if he pointed with his hand

Key Takeaway
Liability for indicating game is conditional on the hunter's reliance on the indication and his lack of prior knowledge; cooperation in killing results in shared liability.

Liability of a Muhrim for Indicating Game
Expansion of Al-Quduri on muhrim's indication

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a muhrim is liable for compensation when indicating game to another, and apply them to different scenarios.

Core Idea
A muhrim is liable for game killed through his indication only if the person follows his lead and captures the game.

A pilgrim in ihram must avoid hunting or causing game to be hunted. But what if he merely points or speaks about game's location? The Hanafi school provides detailed rules distinguishing between pointing (ishara) and verbal indication (dalala), and conditions that trigger liability. This lesson unpacks those rules, showing when a muhrim must pay compensation (jaza) and when he is exempt.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish ishara from dalala
Identify that 'pointing' (ishara) applies to present game, while 'indicating' (dalala) applies to absent game. The passage treats them separately, but both can incur liability.
Ishara is by hand; dalala is by speech.
2
Extract the general rule for dalala
If a muhrim says to a non-muhrim 'Behind this wall is game' and the non-muhrim finds and kills several animals, the muhrim is liable for all. This establishes that the indication must be the direct cause of the killing.
Counterexample: if the indication mentions only one animal but more are found, liability is only for the indicated one.
3
List the three conditions for liability
From the text, liability requires: (1) The indication is followed by actual taking (qabd) of the game; (2) The person indicated did not already know the game's location; (3) The person believes the indication and follows it (i.e., acts upon it).
If any condition fails, no compensation is due from the first indicator.
4
Apply the conditions to the scenario of multiple game
When a single indication leads to multiple game, liability covers all if the conditions are met. But if the indication specifies a single animal, liability is limited to that animal.
The text contrasts 'صيود كثيرة' (many game) with 'صيد واحد' (one game).
5
Consider the scenario of aiding with a weapon
If a muhrim provides another muhrim with a bow or knife, liability depends on direct causation. Providing a bow and the other kills the game: both are liable. Borrowing a knife to slaughter: the lender is not liable if the borrower could have slaughtered without it.
There is a difference of opinion on the knife issue within the Hanafi school.

Source Text

ولو رأى المحرم صيداً في موضع لا يقدر عليه إلا أن يرميه بشيء، محرم آخر على قوس ونشاب، أو دفع إليه ذلك، فرماه، فقتله:

وقيل : عليه الجزاء.
He said: (And does not point to it): meaning with his hand. He said: (And does not indicate it) meaning with his tongue, he does not say: 'In such and such place is game.' So pointing is specific to presence, and indication to absence. If a muhrim says to a non-muhrim: 'Behind this wall is game,' and then there are many game animals, so he takes them and kills them: then the one who indicated is liable for all of it. Contrary to the case if he saw one game animal and indicated it to him, and then there are other game animals with it, and the person indicated kills them: then the one who indicated is only liable for the game he indicated. Then indication only applies if it is followed by taking, and that the person indicated is not already aware of the location of the game, and that he believes his indication and follows his trace. But if he disbelieves him in the indication and does not follow his trace until another indicates to him, and he believes him and follows his trace and kills it: then there is no compensation on the first indicator. He indicated to him. And if a muhrim sees game in a place he cannot reach except by throwing something, and another muhrim (uses) a bow and arrow, or gives him that and he throws and kills it: then each of them is liable. And if a muhrim borrows a knife from a muhrim to slaughter game with it, and he slaughters the game: then there is no compensation on the owner of the knife. And it is said: he is liable.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn of Al-Quduri regarding the prohibitions of ihram. It discusses the ruling on a muhrim who guides or directs another person to game, leading to its killing. The author distinguishes between pointing (ishara) and indicating (dalala). Pointing is for present game, while indication is for absent game. The general rule is that if a muhrim tells a non-muhrim about game and the non-muhrim kills it, the muhrim is liable for compensation (jaza) for all the game killed as a result of that indication. However, if the muhrim only indicates one animal but the non-muhrim finds and kills additional animals, the muhrim is only liable for the one he indicated. The liability for indication is conditional on several factors: the indication must be followed by actual taking (qabd), the person indicated must not already know the location, and he must believe the indication and follow it. If the person does not follow the first indication but later follows a second person's indication, the first is not liable. Then the text discusses scenarios where a muhrim assists by providing a weapon or borrowing a knife. In the case of borrowing a knife, there are two opinions: the first says no compensation on the lender, the second says yes. The text does not resolve which is stronger here, but a later note (page 334) says the first applies if the borrower was capable of slaughtering, the second if not.

Key Terms
جزاء (jazā’): Compensation or penalty owed for killing game while in ihram; typically a sacrifice (dam) or equivalent.
دلالة (dalālah): Verbal indication or description of game's location by a muhrim to another person.
قبض (qabḍ): Taking possession or capture; a condition for liability on the indicator.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) One compensation for each animal killed
C) No compensation because the indication was vague
D) Only if the non-muhrim believed the muhrim

Key Takeaway
A muhrim is liable for game killed through his indication only if the person follows his lead and captures the game.

Prohibited Clothing for the Muhrim and the Exception for Women
Lists prohibitions and exceptions for ihram clothing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the specific garments prohibited for a male muhrim, apply the condition for wearing khuff, and explain why women are exempt from the prohibition of sewn garments.

Core Idea
Four garments are prohibited for a male muhrim; women may wear sewn clothes but must not cover the face.

The state of ihram brings specific restrictions on clothing. A male muhrim must avoid certain garments that are tailored or customarily worn. This lesson lists those items and the exceptional permission for women, supported by the hadith 'Ihram of a woman is in her face.'

Guided Path
1
Identify prohibited garments for men
List the items: shirt (qamis), trousers (sirwal), turban (imamah), skullcap (falansuwah/qalansuwah), cloak (qaba'), and leather socks (khuff). These are prohibited when worn in the customary manner.
The prohibition applies to wearing them as intended; alternative uses (e.g., wrapping a shirt as a loincloth) are permitted.
2
Note the exception for khuff
If a male muhrim cannot find sandals (na'l), he may wear khuff after cutting them below the ankles. This is a conditional allowance.
Cutting below the ankles ensures the khuff no longer resembles a normal shoe.
3
Understand the ruling for qaba'
Wearing a cloak is analyzed: if the arms are inserted into sleeves, it is prohibited; if not inserted, it is permissible (like a shawl).
This distinction is based on the resemblance to tailored garments.
4
Explain women's exception and its proof
Women may wear sewn garments and khuff because their entire body is awrah and covering with unsewn cloth is difficult. The proof is the hadith 'Ihram of a woman is in her face,' meaning the face is the only part she must actively avoid covering (with a veil).
The hadith is weak but supported by the saying of Ibn Umar (mawquf).
5
Distinguish customary vs. alternate wearing
The prohibition applies to the customary way of wearing these garments. If a shirt is worn as a loincloth (izar) or a turban as a shawl, it is not considered a violation.
This shows the intent (niyyah) in wearing matters.

Source Text

فالأول محمول على ما إذا كان المستعير يقدر على ذبحه، والثاني
محمول على ما إذا كان لا يقدر.

And he does not wear a shirt, nor trousers, nor turban, nor skullcap, nor cloak, nor leather socks, unless he cannot find sandals, then he may cut them below the ankles. The first is understood as when the borrower is able to slaughter it, and the second as when he is not able. He said: (And he does not wear a shirt, nor trousers) meaning the customary wearing. But if he wraps himself in the shirt or wears the trousers as a loincloth, there is nothing upon him. As for the woman, she may wear whatever she wishes of sewn garments and leather socks, except that she does not cover her face, because the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'The ihram of a woman is in her face.' Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage states the prohibitions of clothing for a muhrim. A male muhrim cannot wear shirts, trousers, turbans, skullcaps, cloaks (qaba'), or leather socks (khuff). However, if he cannot find sandals (na'l), he may wear khuff after cutting them below the ankles. The commentary explains that the prohibition is for the customary way of wearing; if he wears a shirt as a loincloth (i.e., not as intended), it is not considered violative. Women are exempt from these prohibitions; they may wear sewn garments and khuff because their entire body is awrah and covering it with non-sewn cloth is impractical. However, they must not cover their faces, as the Prophet said 'The ihram of a woman is in her face.' A note indicates this hadith is weak but supported by a saying of Ibn Umar. Additionally, wearing a qaba' is analyzed: if he puts his arms through the sleeves, it is prohibited because it resembles wearing a garment in its intended form; if he does not, it is permissible as it is like a cloak worn loosely. The line about 'the first is understood...' is a later addition about the knife borrowing issue, not about clothing.

Key Terms
مخيط (makhīṭ): Sewn or tailored garments, as opposed to unsewn cloth wraps.
قباء (qabā’): A cloak or outer garment that may be worn by inserting arms into sleeves; ruled permissible if worn without inserting arms.
خف (khuff): Leather socks covering the feet; prohibited unless sandals (na'l) are unavailable, then they must be cut below ankles.

Key Takeaway
A male muhrim must avoid specific garments; women are exempt from sewn clothing but must not cover the face.

Sewn Garments and Face Covering for Muhrim: Rules and Exceptions
Lists sewn garment prohibitions for muhrim with exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on wearing sewn garments for a muhrim, explain the exception for women, and identify the proof for women not covering their faces.

Core Idea
Men in ihram cannot wear tailored garments; women may wear any sewn cloth but must uncover their faces.

This lesson focuses on the garment prohibitions of ihram, which are a key distinction of the state. The Hanafi school provides a detailed list of forbidden garments for men, along with a significant exception for women due to the difficulty of covering the entire awrah with unsewn cloth. We will also examine the ruling on women covering their faces, a point of cross-madhhab disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibited garments for men
List the specific items mentioned: shirt (qamis), trousers (sirwal), turban (imamah), skullcap (qalansuwah), cloak (qaba), and leather socks (khuff). Understand that these are tailored garments that conform to the body parts.
The prohibition is only for the customary method of wearing; wrapping a shirt around the waist as an izār is allowed.
2
Analyze the exception for khuffayn
If a muhrim cannot find sandals (na‘layn), he may wear khuffayn provided he cuts them below the ankles. Note the definition of ka‘b (ankle bone) as the protrusion in the middle of the foot.
This ruling allows bare minimum footwear while still distinguishing from regular shoes.
3
Understand the two modes of wearing a cloak (qabā)
If the muhrim puts his arms into the sleeves, it becomes a complete garment and is prohibited. If he merely drapes it without inserting arms, it is permissible like a rida’.
This shows that the prohibition targets the complete encasement of limbs.
4
Examine the exception for women
Women may wear all types of sewn garments and leather socks because their entire body is ‘awrah and covering it with unsewn cloth is impractical. However, they must not cover their faces.
The proof is a hadith (though weak) and the legal reasoning of necessity.
5
Evaluate the cross-madhhab dispute
The Shafi‘i school obligates women to cover their faces in ihram; the Hanafi school permits exposing the face. Compare the proofs: hadith vs. qiyas.
The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is that a Hanafi woman may show her face, while a Shafi‘i woman must cover it.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الحج ولا يلبس قميصاً، ولا سراويل، ولا عمامة، ولا قَلَنْسُوَةً، ولا قباء، ولا خفين، إلا أن لا يجد النَّعلين ، فيَقْطَعُهما أسفل من الكعبين. فالأول محمول على ما إذا كان المستعير يقدر على ذبحه، والثاني محمول على ما إذا كان لا يقدر. * قوله : (ولا يلبس قميصاً، ولا سراويل)، يعني اللُّبْسَ المعتاد. أما إذا اتزر بالقميص أو ارتدى بالسراويل : لا شيء عليه. 6 وأما المرأة فلها أن تلبس ما شاءت من المخيط، والخفين، إلا أنها لا تُغطي وجهها؛ لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: «إحرام المرأة في وجهها (۱) ولأن بدنها عورة، وسَتْرُه بما ليس بمخيط يتعذر، فلذلك جُوز لها لبس المخيط. قوله: (ولا عمامة ولا قلنسوة ولا قباء، ولا خُفَّيْن، إلا أن لا يجد النعلين : فيَقْطَعُهما أسفل من الكعبين). لبس القباء على وجهين: إن أدخل يديه في كميه: لم يجز، وإن لم يدخلهما : جاز. (۱) سنن الدارقطني ٢٩٤/٢ ، سنن البيهقي ٤٧/٥ ، المعجم الكبير للطبراني (١٣٣٧٥) ٣٧٠/١٢ ، وهو حديث ضعيف، وروي موقوفاً على ابن عمر، وصحح، كما في البدر المنير ٣٥/١٦ ، التلخيص الحبير ٢٧٢/٢، واستدل به ابن الهمام في فتح القدير ٣٤٦/٢ على أنه قول صحابي. كتاب الحج ولا يُغطي رأسه، ولا وجهه . ولا يَمَسُّ طِيْباً . ولا يحلق رأسه، ولا شعرَ بدنه، ٣٣٥ والكعب هنا : هو الناتئ في وسط القدم عند معقد الشراك. قوله: (ولا يُغطي رأسه، ولا وجهه)، يعني التغطية المعهودة. أما لو حَمَل على رأسه عدل بُر، وشبهه : فلا شيء عليه؛ لأن ذلك لا يحصل به المقصود من الارتفاق. قوله: (ولا يمس طيباً)، وكذا لا يَدَّهِنُ. ولا بأس أن يلبس الثوب المبخَّرَ ؛ لأنه غير مستعمل لجزء من الطيب، وإنما يحصل له مجرد الرائحة، وذلك لا يكون تطيباً. ويكره له شم الريحان، والطيب، وليس عليه في ذلك شيء؛ لأنه غير مستعمل لجزء منه. ولا بأس أن يكتحل إذا لم يكن الكحل مطيباً. ولا بأس أن يحتجم ، ويفتصد، ويُجبر الكسر. وليس له أن يختضب بالحناء؛ لأنه طيب. ويكره له أن يقبل امرأته أو يضاجعها. * قوله: (ولا يحلق رأسه، ولا شعرَ بدنه)؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَا تَحْلِقُوا رُءُوسَكُمْ حَتَّى يَبْلُغَ الْهَدْيُ مَحِلَّهُ﴾ البقرة / ١٩٦ : أي حتى يبلغ الهدي الحرم،
The Book of Hajj: He must not wear a shirt, nor trousers, nor a turban, nor a skullcap, nor a cloak (qaba), nor leather socks (khuffayn), unless he cannot find sandals, then he cuts them below the ankles. The first (case) is interpreted as when the borrower is able to slaughter (the sacrificial animal), and the second when he is not able. * His statement: (He must not wear a shirt, nor trousers) means the customary wearing. As for if he wraps himself with a shirt (as an izaar) or wears trousers as a rida (upper garment), there is no penalty. 6 As for the woman, she may wear whatever she wishes of sewn garments and leather socks, except that she must not cover her face, because of his statement, peace be upon him: 'A woman's ihram is in her face' and because her body is awrah (must be covered), and covering it with unsewn cloth is difficult; therefore she is permitted to wear sewn garments. His statement: (Nor a turban, nor a skullcap, nor a cloak, nor two leather socks, unless he cannot find sandals, then he cuts them below the ankles). Wearing the cloak has two aspects: If he puts his arms into its sleeves, it is not permissible; if he does not put them in, it is permissible. The Book of Hajj: And he must not cover his head nor his face. And he must not apply perfume. And he must not shave his head nor the hair of his body. And the ankle here is the protruding bone in the middle of the foot at the point of the shoelace. His statement: (And he must not cover his head nor his face) means the customary covering. As for if he carries a load of wheat or the like on his head, there is no penalty, because the purpose of comfort is not achieved thereby. His statement: (And he must not apply perfume), and likewise he must not oil (his hair). There is no harm in his wearing a garment that has been fumigated (with incense), because it does not involve using a part of the perfume; he only gets mere scent, and that is not considered perfuming. It is disliked (makruh) for him to smell basil (sweet-smelling plants) and perfume, but there is no penalty on him for that, because he does not use a part of it. There is no harm in his applying kohl if the kohl is not perfumed. There is no harm in his undergoing cupping, phlebotomy, and setting a broken bone. It is not permissible for him to dye with henna, because it is perfume. It is disliked (makruh) for him to kiss his wife or lie beside her. * His statement: (And he must not shave his head nor the hair of his body) due to the saying of Allah the Exalted: {And do not shave your heads until the sacrificial animal reaches its place of sacrifice} (al-Baqarah 2:196), i.e., until the sacrificial animal reaches the Haram.
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage states the prohibitions for a person in ihram during Hajj or Umrah, with commentary expanding on details and exceptions. The text is from al-Quduri's Mukhtasar with commentary by al-Nayri (al-Jawhara al-Nayra). It covers four main categories: wearing specific garments, covering the head/face, applying perfume, and shaving hair. The commentator clarifies that the prohibition applies to customary wearing; if a person wraps a shirt around his waist like an izar or wears trousers as a rida, it is allowed because it is not the usual method of wearing. Women are exempted from the prohibition of sewn garments and leather socks because their entire body is awrah and covering it with unsewn cloth is impractical; however, they must not cover their faces, as the Prophet said, 'A woman's ihram is in her face.' This hadith is weak but treated as a saying of a Companion. Regarding the cloak (qaba), if the muhrim puts his arms into the sleeves, it becomes a complete garment and is prohibited; if he merely drapes it without inserting arms, it is allowed like a rida. The rule about leather socks (khuffayn) is that if the muhrim cannot find sandals, he may wear khuff but must cut them below the ankles to resemble sandals. The ankle is defined as the protruding bone in the middle of the foot. Covering the head and face means the usual covering; carrying a load on the head is not prohibited because it does not constitute covering for comfort. For perfume, the muhrim must not apply any scented substance; however, wearing a garment that has been fumigated with incense is allowed because only the scent remains without using the substance itself. Smelling basil or perfume is disliked but not sinful. Applying kohl is allowed if it is not perfumed. Medical procedures like cupping, phlebotomy, and setting bones are allowed, but dyeing with henna is prohibited because it is considered perfume. Kissing or lying beside one's spouse is disliked. The prohibition of shaving is based on the Quranic verse (2:196), which explicitly forbids shaving until the sacrificial animal reaches its destination.

Key Terms
مخيط (makhīṭ): Sewn garments tailored to fit body parts, e.g., shirts, trousers.
الكعب (al-ka‘b): The protruding bone in the middle of the foot at the point of the shoelace, i.e., the ankle bone.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why may a woman in ihram wear sewn garments?
A) Because she is not required to cover her body.
B) Because covering her awrah with unsewn cloth is difficult.
C) Because the Prophet specifically allowed it for women.
D) Because she is traveling and needs protection.

Key Takeaway
Men in ihram must avoid sewn garments except in necessity (khuff cut below ankles), while women may wear them but must not cover their faces.

Prohibited Acts of Covering, Applying Perfume, and Shaving for the Muhrim
Lists prohibitions of covering, perfume, and shaving with exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the prohibitions of covering the head/face, applying perfume, and shaving hair for a muhrim, and explain the exceptions and proofs.

Core Idea
A muhrim must not cover head/face, apply perfume, or shave hair; but carrying loads and medical treatments are allowed.

This lesson continues the prohibitions of ihram covering the head and face, the use of scent (including perfume, kohl, and henna), and the removal of hair. The Hanafi text provides nuanced exceptions: covering is only prohibited in the customary sense, perfumes are banned but fumigated garments are allowed, and medical procedures are permitted. The prohibition of shaving is supported by a clear Quranic verse, which also extends to the entire body by qiyas.

Guided Path
1
Understand the prohibition of covering head and face
A muhrim may not cover his head or face in the usual manner (e.g., wearing a hat or veil). However, carrying a load on the head is not a violation because it does not constitute covering for comfort.
The ruling is based on the general custom; the objective is to avoid comfort and concealment.
2
Analyze the rules regarding perfume and scents
Applying perfume or oil is prohibited. However, wearing a garment that has been fumigated (mubakhkhar) is allowed, as only the scent remains, not the substance. Smelling basil or perfume is disliked (makruh) but not sinful. Kohl is allowed if not perfumed.
The distinction is between using a part of the perfume (which is haram) and merely receiving scent (which is not).
3
Identify medical exceptions
Cupping, phlebotomy, and setting broken bones are allowed because they are necessary treatments. However, dyeing with henna is prohibited because henna is considered a perfume.
Henna is categorized as a perfume due to its scent, not because of its dyeing property alone.
4
Examine the prohibition of shaving hair
Shaving the head or any body hair is prohibited. The proof is the Quranic verse (2:196) which explicitly forbids shaving the head until the sacrificial animal reaches its destination. By analogy (qiyas), this extends to all body hair.
The imperative form 'la tahligu' (do not shave) indicates a prohibition (nahy) which is understood as haram.
5
Consider interactions with spouse
Kissing or lying beside one's spouse is disliked (makruh), though not prohibited outright. This is a precaution to avoid anything that might lead to violation of ihram.
The ruling is makruh tanzihi, meaning it is better to avoid, but not sinful.

Hajj — Pilgrimage
intermediate
